Country: India                                                                           Project Title: APERP
A: Project Development Objective
1. Project development objective and key performance indicators (see Annex 1):
As in most Indian states, development expenditure in Andhra Pradesh (AP) has been crowded out in the last 15 years by costly
and poorly targeted subsidies, by a rapidly expanding civil service and by interest payments. As a result, critical public
spending on social development and the maintenance of the essential irrigation and road networks has fallen far short of the
state's needs. This is a major reason for the low level of social development in the state, the high incidence of poverty and the
relatively slow rate of economic growth.
As a result, most social development indicators in AP are below the national average. Infant mortality rate is 73 (per thousand
of live births). Population below the poverty line is almost 30 percent. Malnutrition among children aged 0-6 years is
estimated at about 30 percent. The literacy rate is only 44 percent, significantly lower than the national average. At 33 percent,
the female literacy rate is one of the country's lowest. The poor health and educational status of the population is largely
explained by declining budgetary allocations for these services. From 1986-87 to 1997-98, total combined public expenditure
on health and education declined from 4.7 percent of state's gross state domestic product (GSDP) to 3.6 percent.
Against this background, the AP Economic Restructuring Project (APERP) would provide resources to meet priority needs in
human development (nutrition, primary health and primary education) and in the maintenance of economic assets affecting the
rural poor (irrigation, rural roads and core access roads). This investment would be linked to a program of fiscal reform aimed
at bringing about a permanent shift in public expenditure towards these priorities, while maintaining tight control over total
public expenditure and debt. This shift is essential if the state is to increase its growth rate and to maintain the capacity to
finance much-needed human development programs in the longer term. The link between the proposed investments and the
fiscal reform p:rogram would be established by means of covenanted fiscal benchmarks to be monitored throughout the project
period. Specific sectoral objectives and key performance indicators are as follows:
Nutrition. Objective: to improve the nutrition and health of young children, women (particularly pregnant and lactating
mothers) and adolescent girls by increasing the quality and cost-effectiveness of the Integrated Child Development Services
(ICDS) prograim in the State. Key performance indicators: reduction in the prevalence of severe malnutrition among children
aged 0-6 years from the current 6 percent to less than 4 percent by project end; and reduction in the prevalence of moderate
malnutrition among children aged 0-6 years from the current 24 percent to less than 18 percent.
Primav Hea lilk Objective: to improve the health status of the population in the State through improvements in the quality and
coverage of services in selected Primary Health Centers (PHC). Key performance indicators: increase in outlay for supply of
drugs, institutional deliveries, outpatient attendance; adoption and implementation of staffing norms at upgraded PHCs;
establishment of project management unit; number of institutions utilizing Health Management Information System (HMIS);
training of medical and paramedical staff; and NGO and community-based activity in tribal areas.
Primary Education. Objective: to assist the Government of AP (GOAP) to build and strengthen institutional capacity in the
State to ensure that more children, especially from socially disadvantaged groups, complete a five-year primary education cycle
of appropriate quality in districts with a low level of literacy. Key performance indicators: increase in enrollment rate to 90
percent of the 5 to 9 year old children; reduction in overall drop-out rate from about 50 to 10 percent; increase in the average
achievement levels; and reduction in differences in these indicators among gender and other social groups to less than 5
percent.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance. Objectives: to improve the quality of life of the rural population through provision of
basic all-weather road access to villages in the three selected districts; and to improve the management of road maintenance
throughout the State. Key performance indicators: completion and continued maintenance of all-weather road access to about
1.5 million people in the three selected districts; corresponding reduction in vehicle operating costs.
IrTigation Rebabilitation and Maintenance. Objectives: to place the irrigation sector on a sustainable basis through involvement
of farmers in irrigation management and cost recovery; to reverse the decline in the State's irrigated area that has resulted from
past poor maintenance and management; and thereby to improve the productivity of irrigated agriculture. Key performance
indicators: 70 percent of Water Users' Associations (WJUA) functioning by the end of the project period; strengthened cost
recovery for O&M; expansion of effectively irrigated areas on existing systems; yield increase in irrigated crops.
Public Enterprise Reform. Objective: to reduce the fiscal burden of public enterprises and cooperatives by supporting GOAP
in implementing the first phase of reforms and strengthening institutional capacity to design and implement further public



Project Appraisal Document                                                              Page 3
Country: India                                                                          Project Title: APERP
enterprise reforms. Key performance indicators: privatization (bringing to the point of sale) and/or closure of 12 companies in
the Phase I Action Plan; preparation of a Phase II Action Plan; and improvement of institutional capacity for privatization.
State Finances. Objectives: to maintain a stable debt/GSDP ratio; to improve the development impact of public expenditure by
shifting its focus towards the social sectors and operation and maintenance (O&M) expenditure; and to enhance revenue
mobilization. Key performance indicators: lower fiscal deficit, significant change in expenditure composition in favor of social
sectors and O&M, lower untargeted subsidies, smaller and more efficient public sector, and improved delivery of essential
social services.
B: Strategic Context
1. Sector-related Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) goal supported by the project (see Annex 1):
CAS document number: 17241-IN   Date of latest CAS discussion: January, 1998
Reducing malnutrition, increasing the level of educational achievement, improving access to and quality of health services,
reversing the deterioration in inrigation and roads, and putting state finances on a sustainable path, are all identified goals in the
CAS. The proposed operation would be a coordinated effort to meet these goals in one reforming state. As such it would also
support a key strategic objective of the CAS, which is to focus Bank resources on states that have embarked on a
comprehensive program of economic and structural reform. The CAS aims to initiate 2-3 such programs in selected states in
India over the next two years to support this objective. APERP would be the first of these operations, supporting the State of
AP, which is not only among the poorest states in India but which has emerged as one of the leading reforming states in the
past 3 years.
2. Main Sector Issues, Government Strategy and Issues to be Addressed by the Project:
AP's sector and fiscal reforms were reviewed and the policy agenda was discussed in a Bank Report "Andhra Pradesh: Agenda
for Economic Refoms" (Report No. 15901-IN, January 16, 1997). The main sector and fiscal issues addressed by APERP are
based on the policy dialogue facilitated by this report.
Nutrition. Since the Third Five-Year Plan, India has consistently accorded high priority to reducing malnutrition. A National
Nutrition Policy, approved in 1993, sets forth a comprehensive, multi-sector strategy to reduce poverty, unemployment, and
malnutrition, which includes - besides ICDS - food subsidies via the Public Distribution System (PDS), school feeding
programs, food-for-work programs, poverty alleviation efforts and health programs. In spite of this, malnutrition remains a
fundamental problem throughout India, affecting child development and educational achievement and impacting on the health
services and on the productive potential of the population. In the public sector, nutrition efforts are seriously under-funded, a
situation made worse by inefficient use of funds. The focus of nutrition programs is overwhelmingly on quantity rather than
quality in nutrition spending, the targeting of beneficiaries is completely inadequate.
The APERP would address all the issues discussed above through support for the ICDS program throughout the State. Support
for this program was considered during project design to be the best means of reducing malnutrition in the State because: (a) it
focuses on young children and pregnant and nursing women; (b) it promotes a holistic approach to child development; (c) it
concentrates on the poor; (d) it uses community workers; (e) evaluations show it to be relatively better-targeted, more efficient
and having greater impact on nutritional status of beneficiaries than alternatives (for example, the Public Distribution System).
Primary Health. Health is one of the six priority areas identified in the Ninth Plan (1997-2002). GOI has determined that
public investment in health is critical for development and poverty alleviation in India. The Plan emphasizes successful
preventive and promotive activities, better control of communicable and non-communicable diseases, improved surveillance
and improved systemic efficiency. Significantly, the Central Council of Health and Family Welfare has noted the importance
of linking preventive and promotive care with selective aspects of curative care, which is provided at first referral hospitals. At
the state level there are serious issues limiting the effective delivery of health care. Management and institutional problems
faced by the primary health care system include: (a) a lack of coordination of activities undertaken by the Directorate of Health
Services (DHS); (b) a lack of coordination between the DHS and the Department of Family Welfare (DFW) in the delivery of
primary health and family welfare services; (c) a lack of managerial training; (d) a lack of strategic planning capacity; and (e) a
lack of autonomy of the PHC medical officer in service delivery.
Beyond the problems of management, systemic and technical weaknesses are found in the following areas: (a) there is no clear
definition of services provided at PHC facilities, and norms for clinical and diagnostic services have not been rationalized; (b)
the referral system at the primary level does not function well because of the low confidence of the public in the services



Project Appraisal Document                                                            Page 4
Country: India                                                                        Project Title: APERP
available at the PHCs; (c) the Health Management Information System (HMIS) provides fragmented information on a range of
health programs in the State, resulting in ineffective monitoring, irregular information flows and slow feedback mechanisms;
(d) the system for surveillance of major communicable diseases is rudimentary and suffers from inadequately trained staff, lack
of materials and equipment for data analysis and poor use of information for feedback, program response and policy
development; and (e) non-medical services such as procurement of civil works, drugs and other supplies, and financial
management and audit, are weak. In addition, public sector health programs have not performed the community outreach
functions as had been envisaged by the Government. There is a lack of involvement of the community, including with regard
to information on health and medical care, and there are few mechanisms which facilitate community participation.
The APERP would address these problems in a coordinated way. The project would strengthen the existing DHS structure and
the joint functioning of the DHS and DFW. The Strategic Planning Cell set up under the AP First Referral Health System
Project (APFF'HSP) would be merged with the Evaluations Cell presently under the DHS. The project would also strengthen
the PHC medical officer's authority to implement strategic improvements in service delivery at the PHC, and it would aim to
enhance the outreach function of the PHC to the sub-center and community levels, as was originally intended when the primary
health care system in India was set up. Emphasis would be placed on rationalizing service norms, improving the referral
system, improving health management information and disease surveillance and upgrading non-medical services. The project
would also enhance the outreach function of the PHC to the sub-center and community levels, as was originally intended when
the primary health care system was set up.
Primarv Education. Education is recognized by GOAP as a key component of poverty reduction. Low levels of educational
achievement are critical constraints to social and economic development. The priority for GOAP, as for all other states in
India, is to move rapidly toward universal primary education. As in many other states, the key issues in primary education are:
(a) poor participation (nearly two million or about 20 percent of children aged 5 to 9 are out of school); (b) poor retention and
low achievement (about 47 percent of students who enter primary school do not complete class 5); and (c) weak educational
management structures (high levels of teacher absenteeism reduce coverage of the primary school curricula to significantly less
than the mandated 180 days of instruction). In the early 1990s, enrollment in primary education began to grow. This led to an
increase in the teacher pupil ratio to an average of about 1:52 and to an acute shortage of schools and classrooms. As the
State's fiscal crisis deepened during the last decade, education's share of total public expenditure declined (to about 14 percent
in 1997/98), and the share of expenditure on elementary education also declined (to 40 percent of the education budget).
By supporting measures to increase Government revenues and efficiency improvements in the public sector, APERP would
allow a re-prioritization of state expenditure in favor of increased real per student expenditure on primary education.
Specifically, the project would: (a) address the issue of poor participation by funding improvements in physical and
instructional capacity and through community mobilization; (b) increase retention and achievement through Early Childhood
Education (ECE) centers, back to school enrollment drives, in-service teacher training, provision of instructional materials and
other quality improvement measures; and (c) strengthen management by empowering school committees to participate in
planning for (Ievelopment of physical sites and for improving the quality of education in their schools.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance. The key issues in the road sector in AP are: (a) inadequate funding for maintenance;
(b) outdated planning, programming and budgeting methods; (c) outdated design and construction standards; and (d) limited
implementation capacity, giving rise to delays in decision making and in project completion. More than 80 percent of roads in
the State are in poor condition, with only 40 percent of the funds needed for maintenance being provided during the last two
decades. The impact of this neglect has been especially severe in rural areas, where poverty is at its most acute. Of the 27,000
villages in A]', 11,000 are without all-weather road access and are cut off each year for long periods during the rainy season.
GOAP policy is to provide all villages in the State with all-weather access. Recently, substantial increases in funding for rural
roads have provided sufficient total resources for proper maintenance of the network. However, the underdeveloped condition
of the network and the weak institutional set-up for maintenance has caused diversion of maintenance funding to upgrading
projects. GOAP policy is clearly aimed at ensuring appropriate maintenance of a core rural road network necessary to provide
access to all villages, and at strengthening the capacity of the Panchayat Raj Engineering Department (PRED) to execute rural
road upgrading and maintenance, especially at the district level, through introduction of proper maintenance planning systems
and execution of works by contract.
The APERP would support this policy. Its major focus would be on road maintenance: (a) to clear the maintenance backlog on
a core network of arterial roads in three districts; (b) to upgrade and maintain an associated rural road network in the districts;
and (c) to address the weak institutional set-up for sustained management of rural roads by strengthening of PRED (staff
training, introduction of modern road management tools and increased use of the private sector for design, supervision and
execution of maintenance works).



Project Appraisal Document                                                            Page 5
Country: India                                                                        Project Title: APERP
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. In irrigation, state expenditures have traditionally been focused on capital
investrnent, allowing for little attention to maintenance and system management and minimal involvement of farmers. In the
40 years from 1951-90 an additional irrigation potential of about 2.4 m. ha was created in the State, and in the Eighth Plan
(1992/93 to 1996/97) expenditures on irrigation amounted to 24 percent of total Plan expenditure (second only to the power
sector). In spite of this, the irrigation sector is in serious trouble, and with it the entire rural economy. In recent years there has
in fact been a net reduction in the State's effective irrigated area, from 4.3 m. ha in 1992 to 3.9 m. ha in 1995. Currently, the
gap between the potential created and the gross area actually used for irrigation is about 40 percent. The critical issues are: (a)
poor maintenance; (b) low system efficiency; (c) limited user involvement; (d) weak links between the Irrigation and
Command Area Development Department (I&CADD) and the Agriculture Department; (e) inadequate cost recovery; and (f)
inefficient operation and maintenance (O&M) allocations (budgetary allocations are made on the basis of a uniform flat rate of
Rs. 147/ha, about 30 percent of actual needs).
GOAP is fully aware of these problems. A "White Paper" was issued in 1996 with a diagnosis of the situation and, after debate
in the State Assembly, a comprehensive reform agenda was adopted with the involvement of the people in irrigation
management as its central theme. It was decided to launch a state-wide campaign to launch WUAs, with parallel actions on
scheme rehabilitation, maintenance and cost recovery. Subsequent dialogue with the Bank led to a refinement of this approach,
culminating in a policy statement, "Reforming the Irrigation Sector for Sustainable Management and Development", which was
approved by GOAP in May 1998. This policy statement is the basis for the actions proposed under APERP. To date, the
following steps have been taken: (a) a three-fold increase in water charges was ratified in April 1997; (b) The AP Farmers'
Management of Irrigation Act was enacted in April 1997 providing for the creation and functioning of WUAs as independent
legal entities; (c) in June 1997, 10,292 WUAs were constituted, covering the State's entire surface irrigation area of about 4.9
million ha; (d) in November 1997, 197 Distributory Committees (DCs) were constituted; (e) a massive campaign was started to
make the WUAs functional: farmers' conventions and district level workshops with WUA presidents and I&CAD revenue and
agriculture departments followed by a state-level convention of all 10,292 WUA presidents were taken up in May 1998
towards building understanding and commitment; and (f) the Water Charges Review Committee (WCRC) was constituted by
Government Order No. 225 of December 8, 1997, providing a permanent body for annual review and recommendations on all
aspects of water charges and maintenance funding levels.
The APERP would support these new policies. In place of new construction, the project would prioritize maintenance,
rehabilitation, investment in hum an capital and technology. The priority would be to restore the productivity of existing
irrigation systems, ensuring their sustainable management by putting the sector in the hands of users and on a sound financial
footing. This would require: (a) follow-up support to make the recently created WUAs fully functional; (b) training and
extension support for WUAs; (c) investment through the WUAs to rehabilitate and maintain the systems; (d) continued
improvement in cost recovery; (e) improvement in cost-effectiveness of O&M through annual planning and review of O&M
adequacy and costs by the WCRC; (f) capacity building in public sector institutions to support these objectives; and (g) WUA
access to better agronomic and water use technologies.
Public Enterprise Reform.. AP has 39 public enterprises (PE) and over 60 cooperatives in which the State Government is the
dominant shareholder. They receive direct and indirect subsidies of over 1 percent of GSDP. In the past three years, GOAP has
taken important steps to reform the sector, including establishing a Working Group (WG) to recommend a reform program for
each enterprise and setting up a C'abinet sub-committee to review the recommendations of the WG and submit them to the
Cabinet for approval. The WG has already reviewed most of the PEs and cooperatives. Its recommendations include
privatization of several units, closure of unviable ones, and merger and restructuring of the others. Two companies have
already been privatized and four closed. Others are at various stages of processing. A. strategic options study for Singareni (a
large coal mining company) has been completed by consultants under DFID funding. The results would be presented to GOAP
in late May. A State Renewal Fund was established in 1995 to finance severance pay under a voluntary retirement scheme
(VRS) and other retrenchment benefits. Over 2,000 employees have already been separated under this scheme. Weaknesses in
the institutional framework for privatization and lack of resources to finance severance costs are two factors which are likely to
hinder progress in implementing the reforms in future. GOAP's reform strategy focuses particularly on these issues to
accelerate the reform process.
GOAP is adopting a gradual and phased approach to PE reform in order to build both institutional capacity and public support
for the reform process by demonstrating the benefits of reform as well as the Government's ability to contain and mitigate
social trauma that sometimes accompanies reform. Accordingly, the PE reform program would have three components to be
implemented over a period of five years: (a) implementation of the Phase I Action Plan which includes privatization (bringing
to the point of sale) and closure of twelve medium-size/small companies, and a social safety-net program for the affected
workers; (b) strengthening of institutional capacity to plan and implement the PE reform program under technical assistance;
and (c) preparation of the Phase II Action Plan for the remaining companies and cooperatives. APERP would support Phase I
through financing part of the severance cost. The social safety-net program and an institutional capacity building program



Project Apprsisal Document                                                               Page 6
Country: India                                                                           Project Title: APERP
would be supported with technical assistance under parallel financing from the Department for International Development
(DFID) of the UK Government.
Fiscal Issues. With a population of 72 million, AP is one of India's largest states. Despite its rich resource base, economic
development has been substantially below its potential. An annual growth rate of 4.6 percent in gross state domestic product
(GSDP) in the past 10 years has been below the national average (5.6 percent), and correspondingly the State's social
indicators have also lagged. At 33 percent, the female literacy rate is one of the lowest in the country. The poor performance
is the result of large public resources being absorbed by poorly targeted subsidies and public sector employment. Until
recently, total explicit and implicit subsidies amounted to about 7 percent of GSDP, with power and food subsidies (two-thirds
of the total) being the costliest. Salaries and pensions amount to about half of total state revenue (8.3 percent of GSDP).
Expenditure on education, health, and essential O&M have fallen substantially in the past ten years. AP's fiscal problems have
been exacerbated in the recent past by the introduction of an enhanced rice subsidy scheme and full prohibition on the sale of
alcoholic beverages, both introduced in December 1994. The State's debt to GSDP ratio increased from 19.5 percent in 1991-
92 to 27 percent: in 1995-96, and interest payments rose from 12 to 16 percent of state revenue.
The new Government, which took office in December 1995, has taken several difficult decisions to reverse these trends,
including reductions in the rice subsidy and in public sector employment, partial relaxation of prohibition on the sale of
alcoholic beverages, introduction of a public enterprise reform which has so far achieved privatization of two public enterprises
and closure of four unviable companies, a threefold increase in irrigation charges and enactment of a Power Sector Reform Bill
which would trigger a series of reforms in the sector. These measures are summarized in Annexes 11 and 12. They were a
sharp break from earlier populist policies and a significant step towards redefining the role of government - reducing it where
it can be efficiently replaced by the private sector, and increasing it in essential public services such as primary education,
primary health, and road maintenance. As a result, the overall fiscal situation has improved markedly in the past three years.
The fiscal deficit has been brought down from 3.8 percent of GSDP in 1994-95 to an estimated 3.0 percent in 1997-98. The
revenue deficit and the primary deficit have also been reduced from 1.2 percent and 1.8 percent of GSDP to 0.3 percent and 0.7
percent, respectively, in the same period. The business community has reacted positively to these measures; AP has emerged
as one of the top states in India for location of new investment in the country. The economy has responded positively; the
growth rate of GSDP has accelerated to about 6 percent per year
GOAP's fiscal restructuring program for the next five years aims to broaden and deepen the reforms already introduced. The
objectives of the program are: to stabilize the ratios of debt to GSDP and interest payments to revenue, which would require the
fiscal deficit to be reduced to generate primary surplus; to change the composition of expenditure markedly towards growth-
enhancing expenditure and improve its management; and to strengthen revenue mobilization through tax simplifications, base-
broadening, adrninistrative improvements and stronger efforts at cost recovery. The specific measures include: further
reduction in staff in State Government, better targeting of rice subsidies; improvement in expenditure management; preparation
for introduction of a value added tax (VAT); and strengthening of cost recovery in irrigation. The annual fiscal targets and the
policy benchmarks are presented in Annex 11 and 12.
C: Project Description Summary

financed by IBRD, IDA, GOI, GOA.' and beneficiaries. The Public Enterprise Reform component would also be supported by
a parallel Technical Assistance Grant of US$3.8 million from the Department For International Development (DFID) of the
UK govemment. DFID would also finance TA for the introduction of VAT, up to US$4.1 million.

2. Key Policy and Institutional Reforms Supported by the Project:
Fiscal Reforms, The fiscal targets for the project period of five years are to: (a) reduce the fiscal deficit by 0.5 percentage
points in the program period from the estimated 3.0 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 according to the following annual targets; 3.0
percent in 1998-99, 3.4 percent in 1999-00, 2.9 percent in 2000-01, 2.7 percent in 2001-02, and 2.5 percent in 2002-03; (b)
achieve positive revenue balance of 0.4 percent of GSDP in 2000-01 and keep it at least at that level thereafter; (c) keep the
guarantees/GSDP ratio at or below the present average 9 percent; (d) reduce employment in the State Government (excluding
primary education) by 1.9 percent a year; (e) reduce the salary/GSDP ratio from the estimated 5.3 percent in 1997-98 to 5.0
percent in 2002-03; (f) increase total combined expenditure in primary education and primary health from the estimated 0.9
percent GSDP in 1997-98 to 1.8 percent in 2002-03 (approximately 0.18 points per year); (g) increase expenditure on non-
salary O&M from the estimated 1.2 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 2.0 percent by the Mid-Term Review (September 2000)
and maintain it at least at that level; (h) increase expenditure on capital outlays (excluding net loans and advances) from the
estimated 1. I percent of GSDP to 2.0 percent by Mid-Term Review and maintain it at least at that level thereafter; (i) improve
targeting of the rice subsidy by eliminating illegally held ration cards; and (j) increase the collection rate of irrigation charges at
least to 90 percent by March 2001. The project would also support institutional strengthening of expenditure management and



Project Appraisal Document                                                               Page 8
Country: India                                                                           Project Title: APERP
improving administrative capacity for introduction of VAT, both under technical assistance.
A Mid-Term Review would be held in the period following the AP state elections (expected in November 1999) and would
allow the Bank and the newly-formed Administration to undertake a fundamental review of the policy framework and of
specific measures needed to maintain the thrust of fiscal reform during the remaining period of the project. At that time,
GOAP would be committed to present to the Bank a detailed set of fiscal measures, and continued implementation of the
project would be contingent on these proposals being satisfactory to the Bank.
Prima  Education. Policy objectives include: (a) preparation of annual work plans and budgets; (b) reduction of the state-wide
student teacher ratio from 52:1 to 45:1 by 2002; and (c) deployment of teachers according to state norms, particularly in rural
areas. Associated institutional goals include: (d) strengthening mandal resource centers (MRC) and teacher centers (TC); (e)
training school committee members; (f) empowering school committees to enhance teacher performance and student
participation; and (h) improving staff numbers and quality in state and district offices.
Primary Health.. Key policy issues related to financing and cost recovery are being addressed under the APFRHSP. The main
additional policy objectives under the APERP would be to strengthen the primary health care delivery system and to integrate it
with the first referral health system. To achieve these objectives, the project would support the following institutional goals:
(a) establishment of an institutional structure to coordinate activities of DHS, DFW and APVVP; (b) establishment of district
health committees (DHCs) to coordinate and monitor all primary health activities at the district level and below; (c)
strengthening capacity for planning in the health sector; (d) improved fimancial management capacity within the DHS; (e)
strengthening and rationalization of the referral system between sub-centers, PHCs and secondary hospitals; (f) setting up a
health management information system (HMIS) within the DOHFW; (g) strengthening the disease surveillance system; and (h)
greater participation of community- based organizations in delivery of outreach services.
Nutrition. The central policy goal in the ICDS component would be to enhance the financial norms for ICDS training
throughout the State. Associated institutional needs would be: (a) a systemic approach to training needs assessment; (b)
increased training capacity for induction and refresher training; (c) revision of curricula, with an emphasis on technical
training; (d) extensive training for all field staff; (e) formation of quality improvement teams; (f) continuous social assessment
and impact analysis; and (g) greater coordination of services provided by different ministries and departments (Health,
Education, Nutrition).
Rural Road Up,grading and Maintenance. The policy framework for the roads sector is already being addressed through the AP
State Highway Project. This would be reinforced under the APERP with the issuance of a new Rural Roads Policy Paper. The
basic policy goals are to: (a) concentrate resources by defining a core network of rural roads based on district master plans; (b)
adopt functionaLl standards restricting black-topping only to roads where traffic has reached an economically justifiable
threshold; (c) allocate adequate funding for maintenance of the core network through rural roads maintenance (RRM) budget;
and (d) legalize voluntary land contributions for road construction. Associated institutional goals of the project would be to:
(e) set up an Inter-Agency Road Coordination Committee linking the Finance Department, RBD, PRED and other agencies
active in the sector; (f) introduce systematic road management; (g) strengthen PRED's contract management capacity; (h)
improve quality control, including the use of consultants for supervision; and (i) create community road associations for
development of non-core roads.
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. Policy goals in the irrigation sector are to: (a) strengthen revenue collection and
increase efficiency of operations for greater financial self-sufficiency for WUAs; (b) form a Water Charges Review Committee
(WCRC) to review and (eventually) determine water tariffs and to determine needs for O&M (timing of full autonomy still to
be decided); (c) agree on indicative targets for revenue collection by March each year; (d) introduce 15 percent beneficiary
contribution to rehabilitation works in areas covered by WUAs (this would amount to an average contribution of 8 percent
measured over the entire system); (e) prepare annual maintenance plans by March each year, and separate allocations for
maintenance to cover 100 percent of maintenance costs; (f) make progress (on a pilot basis) towards introducing volumetric
charges to be collected by WUAs. Associated institutional goals would be to: (g) support the WUAs, including state-wide
training of WUA officials, NGOs and Government staff; (h) strengthen I&CADD and Agriculture Department to provide more
effective technology transfer to small farmers through WUAs; (i) establish modern communication and management
information systems linking WUAs with district, regional and state authorities; and (j) carry out an institutional study which
inter-alia would determine further options for evolution of I&CAD and WCRC.
Public Enterprise Reform. APERP would support: (a) implementation of the Phase I Action Plan which includes privatization
or closure of 1:2 enterprises; and (b) preparation of the Phase II Action Plan for additional enterprises and cooperatives. Local



Project Appraisal Document                                                              Page 9
County: India                                                                           Project Title: APERP
institutional capacity would need to be strengthened to carry out both programs successfully. A technical assistance program
has also been developed for capacity building, which includes establishment of an implementation unit (IU) within the Public
Enterprise Management Bureau (PEMB). Supported with a core team and consultants, the IU would serve as a technical
secretariat for the Cabinet Sub-Committee which is responsible for the design and implementation of the PE reform and
privatization program.
3. Benefits and Target Population:
The benefits of the APERP would be heavily concentrated on socially disadvantaged groups and the rural poor. While the
whole State would benefit from faster economic growth, arising from greater fiscal discipline in the public sector, the primary
objective would be an infusion of resources into human development and into removing the key bottlenecks to enhanced rural
growth. More specifically, direct benefits and target population can be defined for the main components as follows:
Primary Education. The project would benefit approximately 7.9 million children aged 5 to 9 residing in 14 educationally
disadvantaged districts of AP, a state which stands twenty-fifth in literacy among 31 states and union territories. Socially
disadvantaged groups which have low rates of school participation and attainment would be the main beneficiaries, particularly
girls and SC and ST children. The potential benefits of improving the quality and quantity of primary education can be
summarized as follows: (a) economic: the strong positive correlation between primary education and economic achievement is
well documented; (b) poverty alleviation: differences in educational development across Indian states have been shown in Bank
poverty studies to be a major cause of variations in poverty; the benefits of increased access and retention in APERP would be
concentrated on children of the poorest families; (c) public health: health and family welfare surveys for India suggest that
across all states primary schooling of mothers is associated with reductions in child mortality of 40 percent, fertility of 25
percent and severe child malnutrition of 33 percent; and (d) cost reduction: documentation for the District Primary Education
Projects II (28760-IN) and III (30120-IN) and the Uttar Pradesh Basic Education Project 11 (30130-IN) demonstrated that
halving dropout and repetition rates would result in a reduction of cost per primary school graduate of around 35 percent;
currently, completion rates in AP average only 53 percent for the five-year primary cycle.
Primarv Health. A major benefit of the proposed project is that it would assist AP to put in place a coherent and effective
health system at the primary level and to complement the investments already made at the first referral level under the
APFRHSP. This would indirectly benefit the State's population as a whole by reducing mortality, morbidity and disability and
thus increase the earning potential of the poor. Improvements in management effectiveness, health planning capacity, and a
larger role for the private sector at the primary level would enhance the effectiveness and quality of services. There would also
be substantial post savings through the implementation of streamlined service norms and rationalization of services since
treatment for many conditions would now be provided at lower level facilities. In addition, there would be other qualitative
benefits which would have a significant impact on the health system at the primary level. For example, an adequately
functioning referral mechanism would improve the effectiveness of the primary health care level and encourage a greater
participation of the private sector in health care. Moreover, there are considerable externalities associated with reducing public
health hazards through improvements in the surveillance system for majority communicable diseases.
Nutrition. Direct beneficiaries of services at nutrition centers would be children aged 0-6 years, women of reproductive age
and adolescent girls from deprived households. The total number of ICDS blocks to be covered during the project period
would be 250 (108 old, and 142 new) out of a total of 330 blocks in the State. The project would cover approximately 25,000
anganwadi centers and would provide services to over 2.1 million direct beneficiaries. The potential benefits from nutrition
programs in India can be summarized as follows: (a) economic: impact of reduced frailty and mortality on labor productivity;
(b) poverty reduction: ICDS centers are mainly located in rural areas where over 40 percent of the population are defined as
being below the poverty line; (c) fbcus on women and children: the project would improve access to centers for around 40
percent of young children and pregnant and lactating mothers in AP; (d) public health: a study by the National Institute of
Nutrition across four states demonstrated higher immunization rates for children in ICDS blocks than in non-ICDS blocks.
while the health-seeking behavior of mothers from ICDS blocks in cases of common childhood illnesses was more effective;
and (e) educational achievement: studies of the impact of the ICDS program in AP and in other states show higher cognitive
development and learning achieveiment in primary schools, lower school dropout rates and higher performance in recognition
and perception tests.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance The main beneficiaries would be the population of 2,677 villages (estimated at about
1.5 million people) in the three selected districts which are currently not accessible by motorized transport during the monsoon
season. The potential benefits to be derived from the project can be summarized as: (a) economic: better connectivity with
national highways and lower freight and passenger transport costs, impacting fares, the price of goods, the marketing of



Project Appraisal 'Document                                                          Page 10
Country: India                                                                       Project Title: APERP
products; (b) improved quality of life for the rural population in the selected districts: access to education, health services and
commercial facilities; (c) economic integration: better access to jobs for rural population, better use of existing assets including
existing vehicle capacity; and (d) cost reduction: better value for money in state spending on roads through improved
contracting, management and planning.
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. The Minimum Rehabilitation Program would benefit all major and medium
irrigation schemes, with a combined total command area of 2.45 m. ha. The Recurrent Maintenance Program would benefit all
major, medium, and minor irrigation schemes, with a cornbined command area of 3.7 m. ha. Institutional support under the
project would benefit all public irrigation schemes of the State totaling about 4.9 m. ha. In all, the project would benefit about
2.45 million farming units corresponding to a rural population of about 12 million people, mostly poor. The potential benefits
would be: (a) expansion of irrigation in the command areas, resulting from greater efficiency of water use and reduced losses;
(b) agricultural intensification on irrigated land resulting from more reliable water supply and upgraded extension services to
WUAs; (c) more equitable distribution of water, resulting in significant poverty reduction among tail-end users; (d) improved
fiscal performance by the State, resulting from the hand-over of routine maintenance to WUAs, increased cost recovery and
more needs-based fiscal allocation for rehabilitation; and (e) improved sustainability, resulting from up-graded management,
planning and technical skills in I&CADD and the WUAs. In the longer term, progress towards more generalized
modernization of irrigation infrastructure and technology in the State would result from the pilot operation (Scheme
Improvement and Farmer Turnover), which would involve more intensive investment, initially on a very restricted area.
4. Institutional and Implementation Arrangements:
Overall Project Management A Project Management Unit (PMtJ) would be established in the Finance and Planning
Department, GOAP. This unit would be responsible for all interaction between the Bank and GOAP and for coordination
between government departments and other agencies responsible for project implementation. A key function of the PMU
would be to monitor the progress of policy reforms, fiscal and sector, that are fundamental to the successful implementation of
APERP. Except in the area of financial management (see below) the work of the PMU would be coordination. The executing
departments are all experienced in implementing Bank projects, and (with some considerable strengthening of these
departments) the day-to-day management of the project components would be in their hands.
Financial Management. The PMU would be responsible for providing consolidated accounts, including the accounts of all
project components. This would be a financial management function and not simply an accounting function. The PMU would
have a full-time financial management professional as Finance Controller, who would be responsible for managing the flow of
resources to the project components and for ensuring timely submission of withdrawal requests to the Bank. He/she would be
assisted by appropriate accounting staff and would provide guidance to Pay and Accounts Officers (PAOs) and the Director of
Accounts (DOA).
All participating departments, except the Primary Education Department, would follow the PAO system for making payments
and accounting. Given the scale of the investments, and the need to generate timely and reliable financial reports, the PAO
system would be computerized. The computerized system would be capable of providing component-wise and category-wise
expenditures, actual versus budgeted expenditures for the current period and to-date, the system would also capture and process
information regarding the method of procurement so that limits of procurement laid down in the procurement schedule are
properly monitored. The computerized system would, in addition, capture physical progress so as to map the financial data
with the physical data for measuring the performance of the project. The PMU would prepare and submit to GOAP and the
Bank quarterly Financial Management Reports in a format to be agreed with the Bank.
Flow of Funds, and Departmental Accounts. The PMU would be responsible for release of funds to each implementing
department. It would release funds on a quarterly basis, at the beginning of the quarter, on the basis of requirements estimated
by the implementing department. A separate budget head with appropriate sub-heads would be created in the annual budget of
GOAP to capture all project related expenditures. As regards the Primary Education component, separate project accounts
would be maintained by the AP State Implementation Society. Each implementing department would have a full-time finance
and accounts professional. He/she would be responsible for financial reporting, monitoring district/block expenditures, audit
follow-up, and funds management. Each department would submit monthly project financial statements to the PMU for
monitoring, consolidation and submission of withdrawal applications to the Bank. Considering the number of WUAs (in
excess of I 0,000) involved in the irrigation O&M works, which would receive funds in advance and submit expenditure
statements for works carried out by them, the Irrigation Department would develop a suitable financial monitoring system to
keep track of advances released to WUAs and expenditures incurred.
Audit. Financial statements for each project component would be audited annually by the Auditor General in accordance with



Project Appraisal Document                                                           Page I1
Country: India                                                                       Project Title: APERP
standards of auditing acceptable to the Bank. The audit of project accounts would include an assessment of the adequacy of
accounting and internal control systems, ability to maintain adequate documentation for transactions, eligibility of expenditures
for Bank financing. The audited project financial statements including a summary of SOE disbursements together with the
auditor's opinion on it would be submitted to the Bank not later than six months after the close of each fiscal year. The audit
report on the Special Account imaintained by the Government of India would include a summary of SA transactions and the
closing balance held by the Reserve Bank of India (RBI). The audit report on SA would be submitted to the Bank not later
than six months after the close of each fiscal year.
Procurement A complete summary of procurement arrangements is given in Annex 6. All procurement of goods and services
would be in conformity with standard Bank guidelines, and procurement planning and advance bidding documents for all
components would have been agreed before negotiations. Each component would manage its own procurement in accordance
with provisions agreed during appraisal.
Fiscal Reforms. The Finance Department would be responsible for managing the fiscal reform component. It would
coordinate closely-with the other departments (Revenue, Irrigation, General Administration) to ensure that all agreed measures
are taken on a timely basis.
Primary Education. The component would be managed by AP State Implementation Society, AP Prathmik Vidya Parishattu
(APPVP). This Society, registered as an autonomous organization, is already managing the DPEP investment in 5 districts
being supported by the UK Department for International Development (DFID). Monitoring and evaluation arrangements
would include PMIS and EMIS reports, third party evaluation of civil works, annual review of district and state AWPBs, bi-
annual joint supervision missions by the Bank and GOI, and other district-based multipurpose surveys, including learning
achievement of class V students in the final year of the project, and other project research and studies. In addition, periodic
classroom observation studies would document the introduction and impact of improved teaching methods in schools in project
districts. The District Primary Education program (DPEP) Bureau of the Department of Education (DPE) at the Ministry of
Human Resource Development, GOI, would provide the necessary technical support.
GOI would make the proceeds of the Credit available to GOAP, and GOAP would provide funds to APPVP in accordance with
standard arrangements for devel.opmental assistance to the states of India. If and when the primary education component of
APERP was accepted as a CSS, GOI would make the proceeds of the Credit available on a grant basis to APPVP under a
memorandum of understanding to be entered into between the GOI and APPVP
Primary Health. The component would be managed by a Project Director located in the DHS who would coordinate all
activities of the DHS, the DFW and APVVP relating to the Project. He/she would report administratively to the Secretary,
Medical and Health. A Project Governing Board (PGB), including high-level representation from all relevant parts of the State
Government associated with the project would be chaired by the Chief Secretary, GOAP. Periodic review of the
implementation of the project would be done by a Project Implementation Committee (PIC), charged with coordinating
programs under the administrative purview of different departments. In each district, a District Health Committee (DHC)
would coordinate national health programs, family welfare programs and nutrition programs; the functioning of the referral
system; maintenance of equipment; training of staff; quality assurance; surveillance of communicable diseases; and the
monitoring and supervision of alll project activities. The DHC would be chaired by the District Collector and would include the
District Medical and Health Officer (DM&HO), DCHS, other district level officers in charge of national programs and
representatives from other relevant departments.
Nutrition. The Executing AgenciJes would be the GOI Ministry of Human Resources Development, Department of Women and
Child Development, and the Directorate of Women Development and Child Welfare, GOAP, which would also exercise project
oversight. Project coordination on the ground would be carried out by the Department of Women and Child Development, and
the State Project Management Unit (PMU).
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance. The Executing Agencies in GOAP would be the Roads and Buildings Department
(RBD) for arterial roads and the PRED for connected rural roads. Oversight and policy guidance would be provided by an
Interagency Coordination Committee. RBD has already established a separate unit (APERP sub-unit) under the supervision of
the Engineer-in-Chief, responsible for planning, budgeting, procurement, implementation, coordination and monitoring of the
component. In addition, at Secretary level, an Implementation and Tender Committee (ITC) constituted by GOAP under
APSHP would sanction contracts based on the recommendations of the APERP sub-unit and facilitate high level Government
decisions whenever required. The existing divisions of RBD would be responsible for bidding and contract supervision, but
consultants would be used for detailed engineering surveys and designs. The PRED would establish a Project Implementation
Unit (PIU) with a management cell at PRED headquarters and one each in the three selected districts staffed with about 50

percent (125) of the current staff of the road wing of PRED in the three districts. The PIU would be assisted by a Project
Management Technical Assistance Consultant (PMTA). Design and supervision consultants would be employed for the
upgrading work. The maintenance work would be implemented by the PIU. A technical auditor would be employed to
provide feedback on the results of the maintenance component. For Financial Reporting and Auditing on the rural roads the
PMTA would assist the PIU to establish and maintain a computerized financial management system. The PIU would include a
budget officer responsible for the preparation of project accounts, preparation of SOEs and withdrawal applications. The PIU
would also prepare quarterly progress reports in an agreed format covering the physical and financial progress of the project.
Irrigation Relhabilitation and Maintenance. The Executing Agency would be the I&CADD, headed by a Principal Secretary.
The Agricultare Department (AD) would be responsible for all activities related to agricultural production. The Revenue
Department is responsible for the collection of water charges based on the irrigated areas assessed in liaison with the I&CADD.
Within the I&CADD, the Commissioner for Command Area Development (CAD) would be responsible for implementation of
the project. 'The Engineer-in-Chief Administration, Budget and Planning (ENC, ABP) would be responsible for the
coordination of the Minimum Rehabilitation, Recurrent Maintenance, and SIFT components. The Irrigated Agriculture
Intensification work would be implemented by the concerned district agriculture staff. Institutional support would be managed
by two new units: (a) the Project Monitoring Unit (PMU) coming under the ENC, ABP, responsible for setting up, inter alia,
the computerized MIS and satellite communication systems, and (b) the Human Resources Development Unit (HRD) coming
under the jurisdiction of the DG Water and Land Management Training and Research Institute (WALAMTARI), responsible
for the PIM awareness campaign and the training of I&CADD staff and WUA office bearers.
Public Enterprise Reform. At present, the Cabinet Sub-Committee for the Public Enterprise Reform has the responsibility to
implement the reform program. GOAP has agreed that, once it is established, the Implementation Unit (IU) would be
authorized to deal with the day-to-day implementation issues. IU would be placed in PEMB. It would have a core staff
specialized in public enterprise reform and privatization and would hire short- and long-terin consultants as needed. It would
also oversee the implementation of the social safety-net program by a local NGO. Operation of IU and implementation of the
social safety-net program would be supported under technical assistance funded by DFID of the UK Government.
Proiect Supervision. Each component of the project would be supervised by a joint GOI/GOAP/Bank team twice a year.
These supervision missions would be coordinated with the GOAP PMU and with the implementing department involved in
each component. At each supervision, a review would be carried out of financial and physical progress, procurement, audit and
compliance with legal covenants. On the fiscal side, regular monitoring of the State's fiscal progress would be carried out by
the Bank, and a formal review of the fiscal situation would be carried out at least once a year, to evaluate progress related to the
agreed benchmarks. The second of these annual reviews (in September 2000) would be a Mid-Term Review (MTR), at which
the progress of the entire operation would be evaluated and decisions made with regard to possible adjustments in funding,
management arrangements and the fiscal reform package. Before the MTR GOAP would submit its proposals for the use of
investment resources during the remaining project period, and it would provide details of policy measures to be enacted to
maintain the momentum of fiscal reform. The purpose would be to reevaluate the commitment of GOAP to the process of
reform. The Bank's continuing support would be contingent on the policy proposals being satisfactory to the Bank.
D: Project Rationale
1. Project Aliternatives Considered and Reasonsfor Rejection:
General Project Design. The dialogue with GOAP over the last 15 months contained a clear understanding that investment in
the social sectors and in the State's infrastructure is a necessary condition for rapid economic growth. At the same time, it was
recognized that setting the State's finances on a stable path was essential to the long-run sustainability of each of the sector
investments. This close linkage between fiscal reform and priority investment is the key to the design of the APERP.
At the outset, it was also recognized that the reform of the power sector was the single most important aspect of structural and
fiscal reforn in the State, and it was decided to proceed with the preparation of two operations in parallel, a Power Sector
Reform Loan and a broader investment loan, the APERP. Taken together, these two operations would make a major
contribution to modernizing the State's infrastructure and social sectors, and they would be fundamental to the restructuring of
the State's finances. It was clearly understood that satisfactory progress on each of these operations was a necessary condition
for proceeding with the other.
In designing the APERP, considerable flexibility was retained in determining the size of each component, which would be
dependent on the needs and absorptive capacity of each sector as they emerged during project preparation. The chosen sectors
were identified at an early stage, in light of on-going operations of the Bank in AP and of the priorities identified for the State



Project Appraisal Document                                                                Page 13
Country: India                                                                            Project Title: APERP
during the next five years. In the social sectors, it was agreed that support should be concentrated on on-going programs in
health, nutrition and primary education, where the emphasis would be placed on up-grading systems, reducing costs and
improving management capacity, all activities with a high institutional reform content, direct impact on poverty and a
potentially high economic return. In irrigation and rural roads, it was agreed that the critical needs were in rehabilitation and
maintenance, which have beenr seriously deficient in the past and from which very high economic returns could be expected.
During project preparation, extensive debate took place on the optimum linkage between the investment program and the fiscal
reform. The strategic choice vwas focused on how far long-term investment programs can be tied to fiscal policy conditions,
which may fluctuate over relatively short periods. The outcome of this debate, agreed by the Bank, GOAP and the GOI, was to
recognize that the APERP is a comprehensive investment program, which must be justified up-front by an on-going fiscal
reform program, and which must continue to be justified by the achievement of fiscal and sector benchmarks over time. The
Mid-Term Review would be a critical feature of the project, at which time the entire program would be reevaluated in the light
of the success of the fiscal reforms and the achievement of sector objectives. At the Mid-Term Review, there would be the
possibility of restructuring the fiscal reform program, of reallocating resources between components, or of reducing the rate of
disbursement. In any event, continued disbursements would be contingent on: (a) satisfactory progress to date in the
management of the fiscal reforms; and (b) agreement being reached on satisfactory measures for the continuation of the fiscal
reform program. This understanding is embodied in the legal agreements for the project.
Within the context of this overall project design, each sector proceeded with its own preparation and appraisal, focusing on
investment needs and policy/institutional reforms. The following paragraphs summarize the specific sector alternatives which
were considered during project design.
Primary Education. Technical alternatives considered for the education component included establishing Block and Cluster
Resource Centers to provide in-service teacher training and technical support to schools, as has been done in other DPEP I and
DPEP 11 states. However, lessons learned from the implementation of DPEP 11 activities in five districts as well as from the
UK-supported AP Education Project (DPEP PHASE I) from 1985 to 1995, favor establishing resource centers at the mandal
level and relying on teacher centers (TCs) managed by experienced headmasters for most follow-up technical support. The
possibility of maintaining the present teacher pupil ratio of 52:1 was also considered, but it was rejected as inadequate to
achieve quality improvements, which require the introduction of new teaching methods and continuous assessment of student
performance.
Primarv Health. One option co:nsidered and rejected was to build an additional 800 PHCs which would be needed to bring AP
up to the standard norm as determined by population alone. Building these additional PHCs (while reaching established quality
norms) would require an investment two-and-a-half times larger than currently planned and would not be sustainable in the
long term. Moreover, this would not be a cost-effective approach, since it would not provide for prioritization based on
differences in basic health indicators, the poverty level or the presence of the private sector. Other options considered and
rejected were: (a) strengthening PHCs only where doctors are present (those areas which have the greatest need are also the
areas which tend to be under-served by doctors); and (b) strengthening primary care delivery wholly through contracting out.
The availability and quality of private sector services is uneven across the State; the private sector is less involved in
preventive and promotive care; the capacity of the Government to monitor and regulate the quality of private services is
limited.
Nutrition. The following alternatives were considered: (a) better targeting of the food distribution system (PDS) to improve
nutritional status: this would not focus on 0-6 year olds, the most nutritionally vulnerable; (b) combating micro-nutrient
malnutrition: this has relatively low GOI interest, and the multiplicity of agencies involved poses a major barrier to effective
implementation; and (c) privati2ation of ICDS services: this was not found to be feasible beyond the current training role and
limited service provision from NGOs.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance. Roads are the only possibility of providing access to rural areas (other modes of
transport such as railways and water transport are not feasible). Therefore the main strategic choices concerned cost
effectiveness. At the outset, the option of improving all existing rural roads in the project area was considered, but this was
judged to be beyond the financial and managerial capacity of GOAP. A choice was therefore made to focus on upgrading and
maintaining a minimum core rural road network essential to provide all-weather road access to all villages.
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. The main options considered were: (a) to carry out the full rehabilitation of all the
major, medium, and minor schernes in AP; (b) to carny out full rehabilitation of only the most needing schemes; or (c) to carry
out a minimum rehabilitation program of the largest number of schemes possible with the assistance of the WUAs. The first



Project Appraisal Document                                                           Page 14
Country: India                                                                       Project Title: APERP
option was far too costly in the short run, and would provide lower economic returns than minimum rehabilitation. The second
option would likely result in widespread failure of many WUAs and would not satisfy equity requirements. The third option
was selected, based on minimum rehabilitation in all the major and medium irrigation schemes of AP and support to the WUAs
in minor schemes and progressive upgrading by the WUAs through their annual operation, maintenance and management
activities. This decision would in itself be highly taxing for I&CADD and the other Government departments, and a decision
was therefore made to limit the agenda for institutional reform in the short run while the ambitious support for WUAs is
successfully implemented.
Public Enterpise R-eform Restructuring of the AP State Electricity Board (APSEB) would be supported under the power
sector operation. APERP would cover the rest of the public enterprises and cooperatives. The alternative to the present
program of 12 medium and small companies selected by GOAP for privatization/closure at the initial stage would be a
larger prograrm which would include the larger companies such as Singareni and AP State Road and Transport Corporation
(APSRTC). The present design was preferred to this alternative because AP has very little experience in public enterprise
reform and privatization. With the experience gained during implementation of the present program and with public
support created by demonstrating the benefits of the program, AP would be in a better position in a few years to take on
more complex and larger companies.
