3. Lessons Learned and Reflected in the Project Design:
Primary Education. The primary education component has incorporated lessons learned from ongoing projects financed by the
Bank and other donors aimed at universal primary education. Specifically, it recognizes the importance of: (a) anticipating
enrollment growth and ensuring that it does not lead to an increase in drop-outs; (b) expanding access, coupled with
enforcement of attendance and abolition of child labor; (c) providing access to early childhood centers for younger siblings of
girls attending primary school; (d) community participation in micro-planning, classroom construction, choice of physical



Project Appraisal Document                                                                Page 16
Country: India                                                                            Project Title: APERP
inputs and designs, monitoring teacher attendance and other aspects of school management; (e) linking AWPBs to priorities
defined at the Mandal and community levels; (f) articulating the roles of head teachers, headmasters, TC and MRC staff with
district level authorities to better implement school policies especially regarding number of days of instruction; (g) bringing in-
service training and technical support closer to the school; (h) providing more focused, frequent training to teachers, MRC
coordinators, DIET staff and also to School Committee members and others critical to the successful implementation of project
activities; and (i) strengthening institutions that support management and pedagogical reform.
Primary Health. Lessons from the implementation of the two recent State Health Systems Development projects are: (a) the
importance of maintaining continuity in project management and the key project actors; (b) the importance of empowering the
Project Implementation Committee and the Project Administrator appropriately to ensure timely and adequate flow of funds,
especially when the project is implemented by a government line department; (c) the importance of strengthening management
aspects by providing greater autonomy with regard to management and supervision, adequate staffing of key project
management personnel and strengthening management procedures; and (d) the importance of paying attention to quality
through the development of staffing and technical norms, referral mechanism, clinical and management training programs and
the development of performance indicators prior to appraisal. Key lessons from international experience: The WDR (1993)
based on cross-country comparisons of burden of disease and other data, identified an essential primary care package for low-
income countries as one which provides five clusters of highly cost-effective interventions: prenatal and delivery care; family
planning services; management of the sick child; treatment of tuberculosis; and case management of sexually transmitted
diseases. The proposed project would focus on delivering a similar package of interventions adapted to the specific situation in
AP.
Nutrition. The design of the current project has taken into account a number of key constraints that have affected the whole
social sector portfolio in India, such as: delays in the appointment of project management staff; delays in release of funds by
the State Government to project authorities; inadequate provision of funds by the State Government for supplementary feeding;
a large number of vacancies in project institutions; poor quality and management of training; slow procurement; delays in
submission of audits; and insufficient attention given by implementing agencies to improving the quality of services. Over
recent years, numerous evaluations of ICDS have been conducted, and more specific lessons have emerged, as follows: (a)
better worker training is key to improving the impact of the program; (b) better coordination of health, family welfare and
nutrition services is an essential prerequisite for ICDS strengthening; (c) focus on the biologically vulnerable is critical; (d)
better monitoring and supportive supervision for the AWWs is needed; (e) pre-school nutrition is a key factor in early
childhood development and enhances enrolhment and impact of primary education; (f) all aspects of the program perform better
in states with greater technical and managerial capacity (e.g. Tamil Nadu). Program impact has generally been higher in the
more-advanced, southern states (e.g., AP in ICDS I) than in more backward, northern states (e.g., UP and Madhya Pradesh).
Rural Road U2pgradinn and Maintenance. The following lessons are reflected in the project design: (a) intensive inputs over a
manageable area are more effective and carry a wider impact than inputs spread over a wider area; (b) comprehensive master
plans for rural road development are necessary to increase the effectiveness of the investment; (c) commitment of the State
Government for the maintenance of rural roads is an essential element of success and sustainability, as is the preparation of
annual maintenance plans and monitoring arrangements; (d) a clear policy framework for sustainable rural road development
was lacking in most of the earlier projects and is now an integral part of the proposed project; and (e) coordination between
various agencies dealing with roads is essential, and a coordination committee for all road related matters in AP would be set-
up as part of the proposed policy framework.
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. Irrigation projects appraised before the India Irrigation Sector Review (1991) were
primarily enclave construction projects with minimal focus on irrigation and water resources management issues and minimal
farmer involvement, institutional strengthening, policy reforms or cost recovery. Most performed unsatisfactorily, as
evidenced in AP itself where the AP Irrigation II project was a chronic poor performer, exacerbated by weak initial project
design. The need for strong preparation and design and the need to tackle management and financial issues are lessons learned
by AP and embodied in the AP Irrigation III Project. Under the APERP, project design is based on detailed consultant studies,
and all tertiary level investment would be based on detailed walk-throughs with WUAs. Both AP III and APERP have drawn
significant lessons from recent water resources management projects (WRCPs) in Orissa, Tamil Nadu and Haryana. These
recognize that reforms are needed at the state level with the project assisting its implementation. The best experience in this
respect has also been incorporated from other countries such as Mexico. The most important features are: (a) the piloting of
WJUAs, (b) the engineering features of the WRCP rehabilitation programs; (c) the need to articulate state objectives and
expenditure priorities; and (d) the importance of up-front actions before project implementation, especially procurement
procedures for farmer implemented works and institution strengthening features.



Project Appraisal Document                                                              Page 17
Country: India                                                                          Project Title: APERP
4. Indications of borrower commitment and ownership:
Fiscal Reform Program. Strong commitment to the fiscal reforms is demonstrated by a number of measures taken in the past
two years. First, GOAP issued a number of white papers in June 1996 to inform the public about the pressing fiscal and sector
difficulties the State was facing. These papers were widely discussed in the State creating awareness of the issues and reform
options. Following these discussions, GOAP took several politically difficult measures to reverse the deteriorating fiscal
situation (see Annex 11 and Annex 12). Second, the Bank's Economic Report for AP was prepared at the request of and in
close cooperation with the state authorities. Full agreement has been reached with GOAP on the report's main
recommendations for fiscal and sector reforms (January 1997). Third, GOAP issued two policy statements to describe its fiscal
and power sector reforms to be implemented in the next few years. The fiscal program supported by APERP and the power
sector reform program supported by a parallel power sector loan are based on these policy statements.
Primary Education. GOAP has undertaken the following actions which reflect the strong commitment to the goal of universal
primary education: (a) initiated a comprehensive "bottom-up", micro-planning project planning exercise in all villages and
urban slums in the project districts; (b) promulgated the AP Community Participation in School Education Act 1998 to
empower school committees and local bodies for school improvement initiate the process of filling vacant teaching posts in
project districts; (d) undertaken studies to guide re-deployment of teaching staff to reduce the present wide variations in teacher
pupil ratios particularly between urban and rural districts which it would implement; (e) appointed core project staff at the state
and project levels; and (f) undlertaken many community mobilization and preparatory training activities as well as taken steps to
rapidly implement MRC and school construction.
Primary Health. GOAP has expressed its commitment to health sector development by furnishing a Letter of Health Sector
Policy Development as part of the APFRHSP, the key elements of which are being implemented. In addition, AP has
participated in several workshops organized jointly by the Union Ministry of Health and Family Welfare (MOHFW) and the
Bank on broad-ranging health sector development issues, and has developed this project on the basis of many of the
recommendations from the on-going discussions and dialogue.
Nutrition. The commitment of GOAP and other stakeholders to strengthening ICDS in the State is demonstrated by: (a)
extensive participation in project preparation by central and State Government officials, NGOs, academic institutions, field
managers and workers, local government representatives and clients; (b) GOAP willingness to substantially increase spending
on service quality improvement, as well as to expand coverage; and (c) commitment by GOI of about 28 percent of project
costs, a substantially higher proportion than for most Bank-fmanced projects in India.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance. In the framework of the APSHP, GOAP has already taken a number of initiatives to
revitalize the road sector. Regarding rural roads, GOAP is committed under the project to adopt a new policy framework for
rural roads. Project preparation was carried out in a very short time. All concerned persons, including politicians, planners,
engineers and government officials, are eager to implement the project, and GOAP has already started project preparation work
for upgrading the state of rural roads in the remaining 19 districts of the State.
lrrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. GOAP commitment has been demonstrated over the past two years, first with its
candid diagnostic of irrigation sector problems in 1996, then by preparation of the policy statement "Reforming the Irrigation
Sector for Sustainable Management and Developmenft in May 1998 and by actions already undertaken to get the program
under way. These actions include passing the AP Farmers Management of Irrigation System Act (Act 11 of 1997) (FMIS) the
tripling of water charges, the election of state-wide of WUAs, the preparation of WUA and staff training modules, completion
of the first round of training for all WUA Presidents, and completion of joint walk-throughs of the irrigation systems with
farmers. Project preparation has also been rapid, involving detailed work by I&CADD and AD staff with assistance of
consultants and NGOs. The most encouraging feature has been the political leadership behind these efforts, including rural
workshops chaired by the Chief Minister and involving the combined efforts of the full State Administration.
Public Enterprise Reform. Measures taken so far show strong commitment by GOAP. These measures include: setting up a
Working Group (WG) and a Cabinet Sub-Committee to prepare a reform program for the public enterprise sector; approving a
'strategic options study" for Singareni (a large coal mining company); setting up a State Renewal Fund to finance the severance
payments; and completing privatization of two companies and closure of four enterprises.
5. Value Added of Bank Support in this Project:
The APERP exemplifies the comparative advantage of the Bank. The linkage of major poverty-oriented investment with an
overarching fiscal reform would not have been conceivable without the intervention of an outside agency such as the Bank. In



Project Appraisal Document                                                               Page 18
Country: India                                                                           Project Title: APERP
this particular case, the Bank received the full support from GOI for its general strategy of focusing its resources on reforming
states. The message is quite simple. Scarce Bank resources can be put to maximum effect if they are applied in a concentrated
way in support of state governments which have already demonstrated their commitment to reform. The linkage of investment
with reform in such a comprehensive manner is a unique approach.
Within the individual sectors, there are also circumstances, derived from historical associations, which give the Bank a
comparative advantage. These are (a) in Primary Education, the Bank has become the key source of technical support and
external funding for universalizing primary education in India; (b) in Primarv Health the Bank's dialogue with GOI over the
past six years has made it possible to engage in areas of institutional reform which are important to systemic and broad-based
health sector development; (c) in Nutrition, the Bank has assisted GOI and GOAP in re-focusing ICDS by a transfer of
experience from ICDS I and II and TINP II, and has encouraged innovation, decentralization and participatory approaches to
project preparation and implementation; (d) in the Roads Sector, Bank support has already enabled GOAP to focus on the most
cost-effective way to upgrade its highways and rural roads, by concentrating its resources on a core network, preparing
comprehensive master plans and improved quality control and contract management; (e) in Irrigation, the Bank has a large
portfolio of projects in India which provide an understanding of the issues affecting efficiency and productivity of irrigation
schemes and overall growth of the sector. The most recent sector work in Water Resources Management documents this
understanding and has been a key input during preparation of the AP State irrigation policy and the APERP.
E: Summary Project Analysis
i. Economic and Cost-Effectiveness (supported by Annex 4):
Each component has been subjected to economic and/or financial analysis. For roads, irrigation and public enterprise reform,
economic rates of return have been calculated. For the social sectors, greater reliance has been placed on more generalized
evaluations of the economic returns to investment in human resources, while project design has been focused on issues of cost
effectiveness.
Primary Education, Differences in educational development across Indian states have been shown in Bank poverty studies to
be a major cause of variations in poverty. There is evidence to suggest that the major gains in reducing child mortality, fertility
and severe infant malnutrition occur once the spread of education across the population has crossed a threshold of around four
years per person (AP is currently well below this level, suggesting the potential for major gains in the most critical indicators of
social welfare). Unfortunately, there have been no detailed studies of rates of return to education in AP since 1978. However,
at that time studies suggested rates of 13 and 15 percent for primary and upper primary schooling respectively. More recent
evidence of the strong relationship between educational and economic growth in India has been documented in Bank sector
work on Primary Education in India. This includes analysis of long-run data on rural households and a desegregation of the
sources of economic growth. Cost effectiveness criteria have been of central concern during project design. The guiding
principle has been to maximize access and to minimize repetition. There are strong indications of efficiency gains in the
delivery of primary education resulting from reducing the rates of drop-out and repetition (currently, completion rates in AP
average only 53 percent for the five-year primary cycle). The concern for cost-effectiveness is also embodied in the proposed
use of para-teachers, recruited locally on lower pay scales than regular teachers and under the supervision of the communities.
Evaluations of such schemes in Rajasthan in particular, and in Madhya Pradesh, have demonstrated the effectiveness of the
approach provided that effective training and supervision is given. In particular, teacher attendance of this cohort is higher.
Primarv Health, The background data for the analysis of potential economic returns to primary health care delivery are
contained in Policy and Finance Strategies (Report No. 13042-IN, 1995) and in New Directions in Health Sector Development
(Report No. 15753-IN, 1997). Specifically, for AP, assessments were undertaken during the preparation of the APFRHSP of:
(a) financia]L and fiscal impact, including an assessment of potential cost recovery mechanisms; (b) institutional capacity and
risks; and (c) performance criteria. Finally, the AP Burden of Disease and Cost-Effectiveness Study, which was undertaken
during the lpreparation of the APFRHSP, provides a detailed analysis of the cost-effectiveness of interventions by level of
service, and ranks them by the degree of cost-effectiveness. Most of the services which would be offered at the primary and
outreach levels under this project have been evaluated against those criteria and judged to be to be highly cost-effective. A
detailed exercise has been included in the PIP disaggregating budgetary allocations to the health sector and making it possible
to monitor future allocations to the different levels of the health system.
Nutrition.ik A recent study has attempted to measure the economic returns to the feeding component of the ICDS program by
assessing its impact on improving nutritional status (deaths averted and reduced levels of frailty) and the consequent effect of
this on aggregate labor productivity. This study, adopting an all-India focus, used survey data on the decreased prevalence of
malnutrition among ICDS beneficiaries and best estimates from the international literature on the effects on productivity of



Project Appraisal Document                                                              Page 19
Country: India                                                                          Project Title: APERP
malnutrition. Using conservative assumptions of income and life expectancy, and historical costs, the net benefit stream
resulted in an internal rate of return of 20 percent. Cost-effectiveness criteria were applied in considering whether the APERP
should support ICDS or other supplementary nutrition programs supported by GOI throughout the country (the Midday Meals
Program in primary schools and the Public Distribution System). The effectiveness of the former has not yet been assessed but
an evaluation of the PDS has been recently completed by the Bank and judged it to be inefficient, both in terms of targeting and
of cost. Analysis carried out for the proposed Bank-supported Woman and Child Development project suggests that the cost of
delivering one unit of food uncder ICDS to at risk groups is less than one third of that required by the PDS. The ICDS program
reaches a much higher proportjion of young children and pregnant and lactating mothers (who attend the anganwadi centers),
and it is better targeted towards more vulnerable groups than the Midday Meals Program. The ICDS program itself could be
more cost-effective if, instead of providing supplementary nutrition for all who attend the centers, it placed more emphasis and
resources on growth monitoring and promotion, and concentrated additional supplements to the youngest children and those
found to be growth faltering. This approach has been followed in Tamil Nadu, and a number of studies over the past decade
have demonstrated its greater cost-effectiveness in improving the communities' nutritional status. These principles would be
applied in the proposed APERI? nutrition component.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance. The rural roads component would comprise three types of road works: (a) provision
- of minimum standard all-weather motorable access roads; (b) black-topping of existing earth, gravel, or WBM roads for
accommodating current and future traffic where economically justified; and (c) major bridges with length over 30 meters.
Cost-effectiveness analysis was applied to basic accessibility road work. The number of rural population served per unit of cost
was used as the primary cost-effectiveness measure for assessing the benefits and prioritizing proposed road works. Cost-
benefit analysis was used for black-topping road works and the construction of major bridges; in addition, the number of rural
population served per unit of cost was also used as an assessment of benefited population. A total of 1.5 million rural people
would benefit from this sub-component at a total cost of Rs. 150.8 crores (US$37.7 million), or 132 persons per lakh
Rs.(US$2,500). The cost-effectiveness of these road works range from 50 to 180 persons per lakh Rs. The unquantified social
benefits, which are significant, are discussed in detail in Annex 4 of the roads appraisal document. For black-topping and
major bridges, quantifiable benefits were determined from savings in road users costs due to improved road condition and, in
the case of major bridges, to shortened travel distances for both motorized vehicle (MV) users and non-motorized transport
(NMT) users. The Bank's HDM-VOC model was used to determine the user cost savings for MV users, and a simple method
was devised to estimate the user cost savings for NMT users. Total investment for this sub-component is Rs. 101 crores
(US$25.0 million) for all three districts combined, covering 1,300 km of roads. The analysis showed ERR of 12 to 90 percent
with an overall ERR of 24 percent and NPV at US$10.0 million. Sensitivity analysis indicates that the economic viability of
this sub-component is robust.
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. The irrigation component has been subjected to detailed cost-benefit and risk
analysis. Full details are contained in the project files (Irrigation Component Appraisal). The overall economic rate of return
calculated for the irrigation component is 38 percent, a level not uncommon in major rehabilitation and maintenance programs.
There are significant variations between the constituent parts of the program. To provide an indication of this variability,
calculations were made for a representative sample of major, medium and minor schemes, including .the Nagarjunasagar major
scheme, Coastal Region (428,434 ha), a Coastal Region medium scheme (6,000 ha), a Telangana Region medium scheme
(4,100 ha), a Coastal Region minor scheme (100 ha) and a Telangana Region minor scheme (110 ha). The Coastal and
Telangana medium and minor schemes represent farm areas with the highest and lowest productive potential in the State. The
ERRs for these different schemes range from 34 percent for the Telangana Medium Scheme to about 100 percent for the
Nagarjunasagar major scheme and the Coastal Region minor scheme. Beyond these representative areas, the more intensive
investment in the SIFT pilot area was calculated to have an ERR of 25 percent. By piloting it during the APERP, opportunities
for further increasing benefits and reducing costs could be gained, and thus serve as an input into the next investment plan in
AP. Sensitivity analysis indicates that the economic rates of return of the irrigation component and for the sub-projects are
robust to wide-ranging deviations from the base case assumptions. The major risks associated with the component pertain to:
(a) less than 100 per cent operational success of the WUAs, a key building block of GOAP's irrigation strategy; (b) delays in
implementation; (c) slower than projected farmer response to the investment program resulting in delayed accrual of benefits
and (d) implementation inefficiencies leading to higher investment costs. Overall, the project is robust to these risks. An
implementation delay combined with 30 percent shortfall in WUA performance that would provide a reasonable assessment of
downside risks, still results in an overall ERR of 23 percent.
As regards cost-effectiveness, the most important strategy is the move towards full cost recovery for O&M combined with the
devolution of responsibility for planning and implementation to the WUAs including 15 percent contribution by WUAs in cash
or in kind to WUA level investments. For those works which would remain within the planning and financing control of the
I&CADD (for example, main system works) beneficiary sharing of O&M, combined with user representation on key decision-
making bodies, should also exert significant pressure on I&CADD to seek the cost-effective solutions and to prioritize in



Project Appraisal Document                                                            Page 20
Country: India                                                                        Project Title: APERP
accordance with potential benefit.
Public Enterprise Reform. The economic benefits of the severance program include the marginal productivity of the laid-off
workers elsewhere in the economy and the marginal productivity of the foregone labor cost of enterprises. The former is the
product of the probability of finding alternative work and the net income produced by such activity, taking into account:
employment attributes (age, skill level); labor market conditions; and location of the enterprise. The marginal productivity of
the foregone labor cost of the enterprise represents the opportunity cost of the outlays that would be saved by the program and
is estimated as equivalent to the annual wage savings to the enterprise from reducing excess staff. The costs of the severance
program include severance payments and the training costs. Severance payments include only the ex-gratia or incentive
component of the total severance package. Terminal benefits or legal entitlements (including gratuity, balance in the provident
fund, notice payments and earned leave) are not included. Training costs have been calculated on the basis of an average cost
per employee based on the national experience with similar training programs.
The severanlce program covers 7,246 surplus employees in 12 enterprises in the initial Action Plan for the PE reform for a total
estimated cost of Rs. 1 ,115 million including contingency of 10 percent (US$26.2 million). For the program as a whole, the net
present value (NPV) is estimated at Rs. 140 million (US$3.3 million) at a 12 percent discount rate, and its economic rate of
return (ERR) is estimated at 17.4 percent (using the standard conversion factor of 0.9). The financial rate of return is 35.3
percent with a payback period of 2 years.
2. Fiscal Impact:
The financial analysis of components of APERP has been focused on the project's fiscal impact and sustainability. The
following paragraphs summarize the analysis for the project as a whole and that of the individual components.
Overall Fiscal Impact. The budgetary impact of the project (i.e., local funds needed for implementation of all components)
would amount to 0.2 percent of GSDP during the project period and 0.3 percent after the project was completed. The main
contributors to this budgetary demand would be: primary education which would significantly increase the number of teachers;
food costs under the nutrition component; and increased budgetary allocations for O&M in the irrigation and road sectors
needed to raise norms under the project. The increased budgetary burden would be manageable in the context of the fiscal
reform program. It could be met entirely from the budgetary savings from rationalization of the rice subsidy and staff in the
state government, which would jointly constitute about 0.7 percent of GSDP at the end of the project period.
Primary Education. Primary education in AP is seriously under-financed and on a declining trend, and a meaningful economic
restructuring program must include a reversal of this trend. Between 1990/91 and 1997/98, education expenditures fell from
2.9 percent to 2.5 percent of GSDP. Elementary education receives around 44 percent of total education expenditure, which is
five percentage points below the average for the major states and considerably below the share in other educationally backward
states. The proposed project would have a total cost of US$168.5 million, of which GOAP would be responsible for US$3 1.1
million. Over five years this contribution would be relatively small as a percentage of current expenditure on education
(approximately 3 percent). However, as a condition for the project, GOAP has also agreed to fill all existing primary teacher
vacancies and sanction the additional posts necessary to reach, by the end of the project, a pupil teacher ratio of 45:1 with a
gross enrollment ratio of 90 percent in the districts covered by the DPEP - 14 new districts and 5 districts already covered
within the DPEP program. To implement this agreement would eventually add around 20 percent to existing expenditure, or
around US$39.0 million. Allowing for growth in the school-age group, an increase in the gross enrollment rate from 80
percent to 91) percent and a reduction of the pupil:teacher ratio to 45:1 in project districts would require a further 37,000
teachers, in addition to those being employed through DPEP I and II. This would add around US$44.0 million a year. In total,
by the final year of the project, increased teacher salary costs (outside of the project) would be around US$83.0 million,
compared to total elementary expenditure in 1997-98 of US$192 million. Achievement of this requirement implies a growth of
almost 8.0 percent in real annual expenditures during the project period, or three times the rate achieved in recent years. With
the expected increase in public revenue of 1.7 percent GSDP and the combined expected savings from reduction in salary bill
and rice subsidy of 0.7 percent of GSDP, this objective would be feasible.
Primary Health, A detailed analysis of public expenditures on health is provided in Annex 3 of the Staff Appraisal Report of
the APFRHSP (Report No. 13402). In summary, the public health care system in AP operates within the confines of a very
restricted budget. Between 1990-91 and 1997-98, total expenditure on health and family welfare fell from 1.2 percent to 1. I
percent of GSDP. Primary health constitutes only 10 percent of total health expenditure. The health component would have a
total cost of $70.2 million, of which $58.4 million is financed by APERP. About 30 percent of the total cost is recurrent
expenditure. GOAP's share of the total project cost is $2.4 million a year, which is about 10 percent of the total primary health



Project Appraisal Document                                                            Page 21
Country: India                                                                        Project Title: APERP
budget. Under the government fiscal restructuring program, expenditure on primary health is expected to increase from 0.1
percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 0.5 percent in 2002-03 (see Annex 12).
Nutrition, Overall, the fiscal im]pact of the project on GOI would be small. More important would be the impact on GOAP
from increased food expenditures in the supplementary feeding program. The new centers to be opened during the project
would require Rs.62 crores (USIS 15.0) per year for food costs, on the basis of the GOI feeding norms. Since CARE has agreed
to maintain but not to increase its current allocation of food (about Rs. 12.0 crores), all of the increased costs would fall on the
State Government which currently spends around Rs.27 crores a year on this item. In addition, the State Government has
agreed to provide the funding required to fully meet the GOI feeding norms in all existing centers. The total obligation by the
third year of the project would be around Rs.125 crores requiring an overall increase of around Rs.100 crores (US$25.0
million). This would amount to a fourfold increase in state expenditure on supplementary feeding. The increased budgetary
burden would be manageable in the context of the fiscal restructuring program.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance. The project would have an overall counterpart funding requirement during the five
years of implementation for the State of US$66.9 million, of which US$23.0 million (about US$1.9 million per district per
year) would be earmarked for the rural road maintenance component in the three districts. Currently, annual available funds
for rural road maintenance amoumt to about US$2.4 million per district per year from the rural road maintenance (RRM)
budget. The balance US$43.8 million (or US$8.5 million per year) required for heavy maintenance of state roads and
upgrading of rural roads would also be arranged from the State's budgetary sources. In the medium-term, GOAP intends to
create a State Renewal Fund and fmnance it with an earmarked portion of the Vehicle Tax and the Sales Tax on fuels. This
would ensure a long-term mechanism to generate adequate funding both O&M and capital expenditure in the sector.
Irrieation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. The irrigation component would aim to eliminate dependence on fiscal transfers for
O&M by: strengthening the collection of water charges; reducing the budgetary costs of rehabilitation and maintenance by
devolving responsibility to the WUAs; and by expanding the net irrigated area through more efficient use of available water.
GOAP aims to achieve budget neutrality in the third year of the project. This requires significant improvement in revenue
collection from water charges. Over the project period, revenue collection from water charges is expected to be $96 million,
about one-third of the component cost. In addition, capital cost sharing will be introduced as a standard requirement for WUAs
from project initiation. WIUAs will contribute 15 percent of the minimum rehabilitation and the SIFT works within their
jurisdiction in cash or in kind.
3. Technical:
Primary Education. Achievement of increased participation and retention is particularly sensitive to the issue of teacher
availability and deployment as well as to the provision of necessary inputs. Previous experience in AP and elsewhere has
shown that enrollment growth in government schools cannot be sustained without increased investment in staff and physical
inputs and better community involvement in school management. Improving teacher:pupil ratios is critical to keeping children
in schools and encouraging them to learn. A second critical condition for project success, especially for improving student
achievement, has to do with the coverage, focus and quality of the instructional materials, in-service training and technical
support to be provided to schools. Changes in classroom practices and learning outcomes can be produced by comprehensive,
periodic interventions focused on new curricular, instructional and assessment techniques which can be readily adopted by
teachers working in poor learning environments. Finally, community and teacher empowerment is essential to the achievement
of project objectives. GOAP has made a good start by encouraging community and school participation in project planning,
devolving more responsibilities on to school committees. During implementation, this would need to be followed up by
training of school officials and community members and by ensuring that AWPBs reflect priorities established at the local
level.
Primarv Health. A multi-tier health delivery system combining preventive and curative care works efficiently when the various
tiers of the system are linked through a referral mechanism with clearly defmed norms of service. Under the APERP, norms at
the primary level have been defined, incorporating norms for reproductive and child health as well. This, together with the
norms for clinical and diagnostic services for secondary hospitals which were developed as part of the APFRHSP, would help
establish a coherent system of service delivery from the community to the district administration, with the district hospital
acting as the nodal point under which the system of secondary and primary care delivery would function. The PHCs in the
State are now being divided into two groups, and appropriate norms of service have been developed for each group: (a) 500
PHCs out of the State's network of 1,335 PHCs would be upgraded to perform enhanced services based on norms developed by
the DHS; and (b) technical norms for the provision of basic primary care at the remaining PHCs have been developed. About
450 of these PHCs would be strengthened under the project through the provision of civil works, equipment and other inputs.



Project Appraisal Docun                                                               Page 22
Country: India                                                                        Project Title: APERP
The role of the paramedics at these PHCs would be crucial and would be strengthened by providing them with adequate
training and support.
Nutritio  The APERP would carry the potential for increasing the effectiveness of the ICDS program in AP by addressing
existing weaknesses, including: (a) improved targeting, focused on the under-threes and families most at risk; (b) improved
task and time management of front-line workers; (c) strengthened growth monitoring and promotion; (d) refornned
supplementary feeding programs; (e) improved collaboration with the health department; (f) rationalized purchase and
distribution of drugs; (g) improved referrals of malnourished children; and (h) enhanced pre-school stimulation. Important
innovations would include: adding creches to AWCs; total quality management; enhanced community participation; nutrition,
health and education inputs for adolescent girls; independent performance audits; and linkages with primary education.
Rural Road lJpgrading and Maintenance. In the past, quality problems have been encountered in road projects in India with
regard to surveys, pavement design, detailed engineering, contractor capability, workmanship, and strict adherence to technical
specifications. A major emphasis of the project is therefore the introduction of international best practice to the AP road sector.
The quality of engineering preparation has been addressed by appointing an international standard consulting engineering firm.
Bid packages would be sized, and qualification criteria prescribed, so as to be of interest to well-qualified contractors.
Construction supervision would be the responsibility of RBD engineers under the oversight of internationally-experienced
consultants. None ofthe ruralroadworks, exceptthe35 majorbridges (span largerthan 30 meters), require complex design
or implementation methods. The technical standard for the basic access roads is set at the level sufficient to ensure basic,
minimum road accessibility, which is defined as accessible by motorable traffic throughout the year with interruptions less than
12 hours per event and cumulatively less than 15 days per year. The technical specifications for gravel/WBM and BT are
recommended based on both traffic requirement and local implementation experience. PRED would be assisted by the PMTA
consultants to implement the project. Design and supervision of the upgrading component would be done by experienced
consultants. On the connecting state roads, the heavy periodic maintenance would not require complex design or construction
methods. Under APSHP substantially higher standards of project design, implementation and quality control are being
introduced.
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. Better water management would be generated by the minimum rehabilitation of the
irrigation and drainage systems and by improved O&M with the participation of the users. The technology required is
relatively simple, and a strong sense of ownership would be derived from the devolution to WUAs. Improved water control
would be made possible by installing measuring devices at the head of the distributory and minor canal systems and
proportional modules at the head of the chaks to facilitate water control to the Distributory Committees, the WUAs, and the
individual users. This latter measure would also contribute to better cost recovery by enabling I&CADD and the WUAs to
move to charging water on a volumetric basis. Annual planning of maintenance activities, monitoring of costs, and an annual
review by the WCRC would improve the overall effectiveness of O&M. The Scheme Improvement and Farmer Turnover
(SIFT) (on 1 percent of the project area) would pilot a higher level of modernization expected to be relevant for the next phase
of investment in AP. The more intensive modernization, leading to higher farmer incomes and water control, would be a
suitable investment for irrigation schemes managed by farmers. All minimum rehabilitation and SIFT works require detailed
diagnostics of the systems by the WUAs and I&CADD working together, including participatory walk-throughs of the systems
to define critical needs. On that basis, tailored rehabilitation plans would be prepared for each system and agreed between
I&CADD and the WUAs, including the works and cost-share (minimum 15 percent) to be borne by the WUAs. This would be
essential to ensure that the improvements responded to local needs and were cost-effective.
4. Insttutional:
Executing Aenies The arrangements for project execution (overall project management and separate component execution)
are described in Section C.4 above (Institutional and Implementation Arrangements). This section does not repeat those
arrangements but presents an evaluation of their effectiveness and points to areas of potential weakness.
Pimarm Education. There are four Project Management issues of particular concern. First, the continuity of senior national
and state prqject management staff is central to effective implementation. Frequent rotation of these staff often has adverse
effects on implementation as experience in DPEP I & II attests. Second, it is necessary to staff the SCERT, DIETs, MRCs and
other apex institutions responsible for training and curriculum development with highly motivated personnel experienced at the
primary school level. In AP, DIETs and the SCERT have been engaged in primary school improvement under APPEP for
about ten years and have recruited many qualified, experienced staff. Nevertheless, expansion to 14 districts would pose a
challenge as far as staffing is concerned. Third, to improve project effectiveness and sustainability, there is a need for careful
coordination of DPEP implementation structures at the district and sub-district levels with those of the Department of
Education. This issue is now being addressed directly by GOAP, particularly as it concerns the roles and responsibilities of



Project Appraisal Document                                                                Page 23
Countiy: India                                                                            Project Title: APERP
school inspectors, MRC coordinators, and TC staff for managerial and academic supervision of schools. Fourth, most DPEP
states, even DPEP 11 districts in EOPEP I states, have experienced slow project start-up. One cause is a hiatus in activity
between appraisal and effectiveness despite the availability of retroactive financing. However, GOAP has already made funds
available to districts for start-up activities, including MRC and school construction, which commenced in early 1998, and this
bodes well for APERP activities.
Primary Health. Specific measures are planned at the state, zonal and district levels to increase the efficiency of project
implementation, based on lessons learned from similar projects and institutional risks in AP. These include: (a) empowering
the PGB to facilitate interdepartmental coordination and to authorize the Project Implementation Committee in day-to-day
project implementation matters; (b) establishing DHCs to facilitate implementation and coordination of project activities at the
district level; (c) setting-up a mechanism to empower the Project Director to incur project-related expenditures; (d)
empowering the DOHFW in the implementation of the civil works program and equipment management system; (e) setting-up
a new drug procurement arrangenment which adopts, with minor exceptions, an open tender system, in which purchase orders
are centralized at the state level; and (f) setting-up a computer-based fmancial management system for use and operation by the
PIC which would be capable of providing timely and reliable information to the PIC to facilitate effective monitoring of the
project's progress.
Nutrition. Institutional capacity for nutrition in AP is substantial, having benefited from 7 years of Bank support under ICDS 1.
Further enhancement of Project Management under APERP would include: (a) creation of a PMU integrated with the existing
ICDS program office, and including additional technical staff for nutrition, training, IEC, monitoring and evaluation; and
financing of additional technical support posts at the district level; (b) implementing institutional development plans for
existing ICDS worker pre-service training institutions; (c) creation of additional pre-service training institutions to reduce the
training backlog and establishment of a new in-service training system at district and block level; and (d) reducing the
managerial complexity of ICDS, by allowing the State to adopt simplified schemes for women's empowerment and adolescent
girls, which fit their needs and capacities, and by focusing field workers on a limited number of priority tasks.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance. Limitations in the capacity of PRED, responsible for the rural roads, have been the
principle factor limiting the project initially to three districts. The PRED is responsible for implementation, not only of rural
roads, but also water supply, buildings and minor irrigation. It has a total staff of 17,700 people, out of which 5,100 are
technical staff (engineers), distributed hierarchically from the headquarters, to district level, and to division level operations.
Roughly half of the staff work in the road sector. Under the APERP, 50 percent of those would be dedicated exclusively to the
project. A detailed institutional analysis has been made in the framework of the preparation of the rural road component (see
Roads Component Appraisal). It is concluded that the arrangements planned under the project would be within the capacity of
PRED, subject to the substantial institutional strengthening proposed as a part of the project. As for the RBD, responsible for
the connecting state roads, the implementing capacity would be considered adequate, following the institutional strengthening
built into the project.
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. The I&CADD is already implementing a number of Bank-assisted projects and is
well familiar with Bank procedures. The extent and complexity of the proposed irrigation component in this project is such
that a dedicated PMU is essential for day-to-day management under the Engineer-in-Chief, Administration, Budgeting and
Planning. In order for the PMU to monitor all project activities and to maintain consolidated project accounts, dedicated MIS
modules would be prepared at the beginning of project implementation. The project would include strengthening of I&CADD
in particular as regards implementation of the WUA support program and the minimum rehabilitation and O&M program.
While no major staff restructuring; is envisaged at this stage, effective decentralized management would be needed. Intensive
training programs would be a feature of the project, not only for WUAs, but also for I&CADD staff, NGOs and for staff in
other departments such as the Agriculture Department. These activities would be coordinated by a newly created unit within
I&CADD, the Human Resources Development (HRD) unit. Significant consultancy support and study tours would also be
included in the project. In Project. Year 3, an institutional study would be undertaken to provide a basis for further institutional
change in I&CADD and WCRC.
5. Social:
Fiscal Reform Program. The social impact of fiscal reforms is expected to be positive: (a) faster growth arising from greater
fiscal discipline in the public sector; (b) expenditure in primary education, primary health, and nutritional services is planned to
increase substantially under the project and to remain at a high level thereafter, which would improve social conditions,
particularly in the rural areas; and (c) better targeting of the rice subsidy to the poor is a critical objective of the fiscal reforms;
measures to achieve this objective include: new multiple criteria to be used in both urban and rural areas to identify the
population below the poverty line, and women groups to be involved in monitoring the implementation of the reform program.



Project Appraisal Document                                                                  Page 24
Country: India                                                                              Project Title: APERP
Prim, Ecation. Each village in the 14 DPEP expansion districts has carried out micro-planning exercises to generate
demographic data and information on the proportion of girls, schedule caste and tribal children attending formal schools,
alternative school or ECE centers. Information has also been collected on the number and characteristics of drop outs and
about the incidence, employment and educational needs of child labor. Analysis of these data has been used to devise
intervention strategies and prepare the first year AWPBs. During project implementation, targets would be set for improving
participation, retention and attendance, based on continually up-dated micro-planning data, to guide preparation of AWPBs,
monitor progress, and allocate funds. Insofar as child labor and girls' participation are concerned, the project would encourage
affected children to attend formal school through back-to-school drives, establishment of ECE centers, employment of teachers
from the community, and other interventions. AS centers are conceived as a transitional device necessary for eventually
mainstreami[ng out-of-school children particularly those involved in non-household, non-agricultural employment.
Prima  Health, A social assessment and a burden of disease study were undertaken in preparing this component. Several
issues were identified as responsible for the poor quality of services provided at PHCs: lack of on-the-job training for medical
officers; poor infrastructure facilities; poor HMIS; lack of motivation among PHC staff; lack of interdepartmental coordination
between health and family welfare departments, which resulted in wastage of resources; and poor outreach services, resulting in
decreased access of beneficiaries to primary health care services. Women are particularly impacted by the lack of access to
emergency obstetric care. The project would address these issues by (a) setting up a mechanism at the state and district levels
to coordinate programs of the health and family welfare departments; (b) providing timely access to emergency obstetrics care
through the strengthening of the referral system from the community to the referral hospital; and (c) targeting the poor,
particularly tribal populations, to increase their access to primary health services free of cost. A beneficiary assessment study,
undertaken as part of project preparation to identify the health care needs of tribal communities and constraints in provision of
these needs, recommends plans for delivering adequate and quality health care for members of these communities. The
projecfs tribal strategy aims to increase the demand for primary health services by improving the quality of services and
providing elfective IEC to better inform tribal populations of the benefits of using government health services. In addition, the
project would provide an incentive package to doctors and other medical staff to encourage them to accept assignment in these
areas and increase access to primary health care services for the tribal population.
Nutrition. T'he Administrative Staff College of India (ASCI) carried out a preliminary social assessment for the ICDS and
health components of the APERP. The main findings were: (a) the physical infrastructure of the ICDS system is inadequate,
equipment is limited, medicines in short supply and staffing insufficient; some of these shortfalls could be met through better
convergence of services, but inter-departmental co-ordination was noted to be particularly weak; (b) training is particularly
deficient, especially at the AWW and supervisor level; community awareness/participation is lacking on issues such as
pregnancy related complications, safe delivery, and feeding practices; (c) ICDS service delivery and project management are
particularly weak in tribal areas; the APERP would support an increase in mini AWCs in 29 tribal blocks, and close
convergence with the Integrated Tribal Development Agency (ITDA) would be sought; GOAP has already recruited MOs in
every tribal block in the State; (d) AP has the highest prevalence of child labor of any state. A survey conducted in 1996 under
the instruction of the Supreme Court of India suggested poor school enrollment, especially for girls. The survey also indicated
that more girls than boys were engaged in hazardous trades in the State. APERP would address this issue through improved
pre-school education. It would also support an intensive girl child education project through funding a bridge course for those
in child labor, especially girl children in hazardous trades.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance. The roads improvement program would yield substantial social benefits to the rural
population, particularly in the improvement of access to education, health care, and other social services. A village and rural
household t-ansport survey was conducted in Adilabad and Karimnagar. Preliminary analyses of the survey data suggested that
the levels of road accessibility explain in part the differences in social development indicators between villages with and
without ade,quate road accessibility. No new roads (formation), widening works or realignment are planned, hence there is no
resettlement: work associated with the implementation of the roads component.
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. The main beneficiaries of the project would be farmers with holdings averaging
less than 2 hectares (about 1. I ha in the Coastal Region, 1.7 ha in the Telangana Region, and 2.1 ha in the Rayalaseema
Region). The project would lead to a substantially more equitable distribution of water, benefiting particularly tail-end farmers
who are generally in the poorest group. Based on Participatory Rapid Appraisals (PRAs) done during appraisal, the first impact
of the WUA. formation process would also have a positive impact on equity. WUA presidents tend to be from the same social
and economic background as the majority of farmers (predominantly small to medium farmers and without bias according to
caste). The WUA committee elections, based on territorial representatives for different areas in the WUA command and use of
secret ballots, has resulted in balanced area representation and election of leaders. This is an important change from previous
community development efforts, where representatives tended to be large landowners. Another positive feature has been the



Project Appraisal Document                                                            Page 25
Countiy: India                                                                        Project Title: APERP
election of a few women as W[JA leaders, but this is still not common. To focus on the greater integration of women in WUA
management, a sociological study on Opportunities for Women's Participation would be mounted in early PY2.
Public Enterprise Reform. Implementation of the VRS program would affect over 7,000 employees. A social safety-net
program has been prepared to address the social issues, which would include adequate severance pay, counseling, financial
advice and retraining of the affected workers. DFID has agreed to finance and assist GOAP to implement the social safety-net
program under technical assistance.
6. EnvironmentalAssessment:   Environmental Category  [ A    []B    [x ] C
Primarv Education. The project would not raise any environmental issues. Construction of new schools and additional
classrooms would be on the land donated by the community. At negotiations, GOAP would confirm to the Bank in writing that
no involuntary resettlement would arise from implementing the civil works.
Primary Health. The project would not raise any environmental concerns. Due to the outreach nature of PHC functions, health
care waste generated at the PHC would be small, with very little infectious and negligible quantities of hazardous waste. The
project would, however, improve the present status of health care waste disposal through appropriate disposal methods. A plan
has been developed under the APFRHSP for the disposal of health care waste at different levels of the health system, including
the PHCs. A detailed plan has been worked out for the provision of K-wells at PHCs, designed according to WHO standards.
Nutrition. The project would not raise environmental issues, and no resettlement is anticipated as a result of the project.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance. The rehabilitation and maintenance proposed under APERP would not include any
land acquisition or change in alignment of the existing roads. Very marginal land may be acquired, if necessary, for curve and
flattening. Strict adherence to dlesign and construction practices would be followed to mitigate environmental impacts. The
component is therefore categorized as Environment Category C. There are some sections which require bridges with spans
longer than 30m. Of these, for sections requiring approach realignments, limited Environmental Analysis would be carried out
at the time of detailed engineering design. The purpose would be to identify relevant mitigation measures with respect to
construction impacts such as erosion from fresh road cuts and temporary sedimentation of drainage ways, contamination by oil,
grease and fuel in the equipment, creation of stagnant water bodies in borrow pits and quarries. This mitigation plan would be
prepared by consultants for the detailed engineering design. This in turn would be incorporated in the project contract
document. With respect to priority options, cross drain works would be given high priority to avoid stagnation of water and
hindrances to access.
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. The proposed works would comprise cleaning up silted canals and drains and
repairing existing hydraulic structures with no expansion of the command areas, construction of additional storage facilities or
alteration of alignments. No land acquisition would be required. Any materials such as silt and weeds would be deposited on
the banks of the canals and drains or used by the farmers for spreading over the fields to improve the physical condition of the
soil. An environmental overview concluded that no adverse environmental impacts would result from the project. Rather, it
would be expected to have positive environmental impacts through more efficient and equitable water use and conservation and
through the improvements of the drainage systems which would decrease water-logging and salinity. The only part of the
program with any potential impact on the environment is the pilot SIFT program which would cover about I percent of the
project area. This program would include more intensive modernization works on the existing systems. Again, the
environmental impact is expected to be positive (reduced water-logging and salinization), but as a precaution a limited
environmental impact assessment (EIA), under terms of reference approved by the Bank, would be carried out for each of these
pilot schemes as part of their detailed preparation. Project supervision would approve each detailed design and EIA to ensure
that environmental mitigation aspects and detailed designs were appropriately carried out. Project costs and financing include
the funds needed for this purpose.
Z Participatory Approach
Fiscal Reform Program. The fiscal program supported by APERP is the result of extensive state-wide discussions. In 1996,
GOAP issued a series of white papers to inform the public about the fiscal difficulties the State was facing. These papers were
widely discussed in the State creating awareness of the issues and reform options. These papers were also discussed in the
State Assembly before the fiscal reform program was announced in a Fiscal Policy Statement issued by the Cabinet.
Primary Education. As in other DPEP projects, activities have been planned and would be implemented by both district and
sub-district authorities, including Village Education Committees and Panchayati Raj institutions. Each village in the project
districts has carried out micro-planning and produced a plan and request for funding. The school committees would be
significantly empowered for school management and financing under the AP Community Participation in School Education



Project Appraisal Document                                                            Page 26
Country: India                                                                        Project Title: APERP
Act 1998. NGOs have also played a strong role under DPEP PHASE I in local capacity building, producing low cost designs
and building techniques for schools and classrooms, developing textbooks, workbooks and other learning materials, organizing
and evaluating in-service teacher training, and assessing student performance. They would continue to be involved in these
activities during project implementation. The DIETs, Institutes for Advanced Studies in Education (IASEs) and the State
Council for Educational Research and Training (SCERT) have had a key role in project preparation and are the apex
institutions for implementing quality improvement activities such as the design of new textbooks, supplementary texts, and in-
service teacher training modules. Mainstream academic institutions have so far had a marginal role in project preparation, and
that mainly with respect to organizing and analyzing baseline achievement studies. Significant benefits may be obtained by
increasing the participation of academics and academic institutions not only in large scale survey research but also in
qualitative longitudinal case studies, evaluation of specific interventions and project monitoring.
Primarv HealiLh, The private and voluntary sectors, panchayats and communities have an important role to play in enhancing
the quality of and access to services envisaged at the primary level. The rationale for the streamlining of services provided at
the primary level has been developed on the basis of extensive consultation with medical personnel working at all levels of the
DOHFW. Several components planned have community participation as an integral part, including the surveillance system,
the tribal strategy and the NGO/community participation component. In addition, DHCs would be put in place to monitor and
supervise project implementation activities at the district level, and would include community leaders as members.
Nutrition. The component preparation was carried out in a participatory process with a social assessment that included
stakeholder analysis. The component design for decentralized participatory management of ICDS services in the State
involves: (a) creation of mandal ICDS units (2-3 mandals correspond to one development block); and (b) continuous social
assessment to be carried out throughout the project period with the State Institute of Rural Development (SIRD) acting as the
nodal training/resource center. Training for stakeholder participation in the project would be extended to members of village
women's committees, panchayat members and ICDS program functionaries at all levels. The village women's committees
would monitor key project outcomes and participate actively in civil works construction.
Rural Road Upgrading and Maintenance A two-day objectives-oriented and participatory workshop was held in June 1997
jointly with more than 30 PRED and local government officials. Through the participatory process during the workshop, rural
road problems were identified and analyzed, project objectives discussed and prioritized, and an overall description of the
project established. The participants evaluated the process and outcome of the workshop very favorably. In November 1997,
district level workshops were held in three districts, respectively, jointly with district government officials, district politicians,
and PRED staff, to discuss the proposed district level programs. Further workshops were held with selected PRED staff from
headquarters and the districts, to define the detailed scope of the project.
Irrigation Rehabilitation and Maintenance. Over the past 12 months; there has been a major outreach program with farmers
through rural rallies and workshops at the district and state levels. The views of farmers, the academic community and NGOs
have been integrated in the Irrigation Policy Statement, which also went through detailed review at Government levels and by
the Cabinet. As part of the preparation of the AP Irrigation III project, two pilot participatory irrigation management (PIM)
operations were launched covering an area of 16,000 ha, the objective being to develop a suitable model for PIM through the
creation of WUAs. In all, 32 WUAs were formed covering an area of about 16000 ha. At the end of the pilot operation,
farmers were able to resolve conflicts between head and tail-end farmers, distribute water more equitably among themselves
and, consequently, irrigate about 25 percent more land. On the basis of this experience GOAP enacted the AP Farmers
Management of Irrigation Systems Act (Act 11 of 1997), 1997, which elaborates on the structure, composition, formation,
function, and duties of farmers organizations at different levels, and their relationships with I&CADD. Under the APERP the
WUAs would: plan the distribution of water; maintain an inventory of the irrigation and drainage systems; maintain the
irrigation and drainage systems; and maintain accounts. The objective would be to integrate farmers in all aspects of decision
making. The other two key stakeholders are the I&CADD and the NGOs. Both have been fully involved in project preparation
and would have a key role in implementation. Under the AP Farmers Management of Irrigation Systems Act (Act 11 of 1997),
the primary finction of I&CADD would be to assist the WUAs in the discharge of their responsibilities and to provide
technical support. The primary functions of the NGOs would be to assist the WUAs in their day to day operations, in close
liaison with the I&CADD. In sum, the APERP would support a partnership between the farmer organizations, staff of
I&CADD and the Agriculture Department, and NGOs.



Project Appraisal Document                                                              Page 27
Country: India                                                                          Project Title: APERP
F: Sustainability and Risks
1. Sustainabii(v.
Fiscal Sustainabilitv: Fiscal sustalinability lies at the heart of the project concept. Putting state finances on a sustainable path is
a critical objective of APERP, which GOAP aims to achieve by: (a) stabilizing the debt/GSDP ratio at about 24 percent to
contain the interest burden at 18 percent of total revenue (both below the average of all Indian states). This requires a reduction
of the fiscal deficit from the present 3.0 percent of GSDP to 2.5 percent by the end of the program period; (b) improving
development impact of public exlpenditure by changing its composition to shift resources from salaries and untargeted subsidies
into social sectors and infrastructare; and (c) simplifying the tax system and improving cost recovery to strengthen revenue
mobilization (See Section B.2 and Annex 11 and Annex 12 for specific annual targets and policy benchmarks).
Fiscal sustainability of the project components is equally important. The budgetary impact of the project (i.e., local funds
needed for implementation of all components) would amount to 0.2 percent of GSDP during the project period and 0.3 percent
after the project is completed. This increased budgetary burden would be manageable in the context of the fiscal reform
program. It would be financed entirely from the budgetary savings expected from rationalization of staff and the rice scheme,
which would jointly constitute 0.7 percent of GSDP at the end of the project period.
Institutional Sustainability. Each project component would include substantial training and capacity building activities, which
have been outlined in earlier sections of this document and are detailed in the component PIPs. For the most part, the project
would rely on existing institutions with a proven track record in previous Bank operations in AP. The most significant
departure from an institutional point of view would be in the irrigation component, which would be applying an entirely new
principle of WUAs. As referred in Section E7 above, this approach is already tested on a pilot basis in AP with impressive
results, and it also has extremely strong political support in the State. The other area where institutional risks may exist is
public enterprise reform, which is both politically and technically demanding. To meet the challenge, three intensive TA
programs would be funded by DFID: institutional strengthening for privatization, safety-net program for retrenched employees,
and introduction of VAT (see Annex 2). Several workshops and seminars have already been held with the technical staff of the
concerned departments to disseminate information on best practice and other countries' experiences in implementing similar
programs. More are planned.

3. Possible Controversial Aspects:
The progressive removal of subsidies, the emergence of a tighter control over the fiscal situation and the devolution of
operational and fiscal responsibility to water users would obviously remain potentially controversial until the associated benefits
of the program are firmly established and recognized by the beneficiaries. Nevertheless, the extent of consultation that has taken
place between GOAP and civil society and between the Bank and GOAP provides an assurance that the reforms are politically
feasible and that the investments are manageable. With regard to the internal conditions of the project, the long dialogue between
the Bank and GOAP on the nature and content of the APERP, especially the need to link investment with fiscal reform, gives
confidence that, after allowing for political risks, there are few, if any, outstanding issues which could be of serious consequence
during project implementation.
G: Main Loan Conditions
All conditions of negotiations were met. These included: (a) requirements for establishing management structures for all sectoral
components and project as a who le; (b) receipt by the Bank of satisfactory PIPs for each component; (c) passage of the Power
Sector Reform Bill in the State Assembly; (d) receipt by the Bank of the agreed Letter of Development Policy; (e) approval of an
Irrigation Sector Policy Statement; and (f) approval of technical assistance for preparation for introduction of a VAT in the State.
The basic condition for the project implementation is the continuation of the fiscal reform program which has been in effect since
1995 and which has already seen a significant reduction in fiscal deficit. This condition is elaborated in a series of fiscal
benchmarks set out in Annex II and recorded in the Minutes of Negotiations. These benchmarks are divided into two groups:
the first related to overall fiscal performance (annual fiscal targets and a number fiscal measures to be taken during the project
period); and the second to sector objectives implicit in the investment components of the project. At the Mid-Term Review a
detailed evaluation of progress would be conducted, and continued support from the Bank would be contingent on satisfactory
performance to date and on agreement on a package of continued reform measures.
There are no conditions for Board Presentation or Effectiveness.
Assurances Received at Negotiatrions:
Fiscal Reforms. GOAP shall: (a) reduce the fiscal deficit by 0.5 percentage points in the program period from 3.0 percent of
GSDP in 1997-98 according to the following annual targets: 3.0 percent in 1998-99, 3.4 percent in 1999-00, 2.9 percent in 2000-
01, 2.7 percent in 2001-02, and 2.5 percent in 2002-03. [These targets do not reflect the budgetary impact of the power sector
reform program, in particular the fmancial restructuring plan for APSEB under preparation in the context of the proposed power
sector operation. The fiscal impact of this plan would be considered separately when it is completed and agreed upon with the
Bank. The expected pay increase is incorporated in the projections on the basis of assumptions summarized in para 15 of the aide
memoire of the appraisal mission dated May 6, 1998. If the conditions of the actual pay settlement are fiscally more comfortable
than have been assumed, the fiscal targets would be adjusted accordingly during annual reviews. If the actual disbursement of the
loan was faster than the agreed schedule and the primary education and/or the nutrition components of the project were not
approved as centrally sponsored schemes, the fiscal targets would be adjusted accordingly during the annual reviews]; (b) achieve
positive revenue balance of 0.4 percent of GSDP in 2000-01 and keep it at least at that level thereafter; (c) keep the
guarantees/GSDP ratio no higher than the present average of 9 percent; (d) reduce employment in State Government (excluding
primary education ) by 1.9 percent a year; (e) reduce the salary/GSDP ratio from the estimated 5.3 percent in 1997-98 to no
higher than 5.0 percent in 2002-03 (this ratio may increase slightly in 1999-00 when the salary increase is expected to be awarded
to the State Government employees); (f) increase total combined expenditure in primary education and primary health from the
estimated 0.9 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 1.8 percent in 2002-03 (approximately 0.18 points per year); (g) increase
expenditure on non-salary O&M from the estimated 1.2 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to at least 2.0 percent in 2000-01 and

maintain it at least at that level thereafter; (h) increase expenditure on capital outlays from 1.1 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to at
least 2.2 percent in 2000-01 and maintain it at least at that level thereafter; (i) improve targeting of the rice scheme by eliminating
the bogus (illegal) ration cards by January 31, 1999; and a) increase the collection rate of irrigation charges at least to 90 percent

available adequate land for new construction under Part E of the project sufficiently in advance; (q) make such land available that
is owned by AP and free from all encumbrances and encroachment or obtain such land on a voluntary basis; (r) ensure that no
involuntary resettlement arises from any use of land except to the extent agreed with the Bank; and (s) follow the R&R policy
adopted in the Third AP Irrigation Project if there is any involuntary resettlement under Part E of the project.
Public Enterprise Reform. GOAP shall: (a) maintain the Cabinet Sub-Committee on State Level Public Enterprises and
Cooperative Enterprises, and the Implementation Secretariat with powers, functions, organizational structure and resources for
the purpose of implementing the First Phase of the Restructuring Program for public enterprises and cooperative enterprises; (b)
implement the First Phase of the Restructuring Program by December 31, 2001; (c) formulate a time-based Action Plan setting
out the strategy for privatization of the enterprises identified in the First Phase of the Restructuring Program by March 31, 1999
and implement it; (d) maintain and administer the State Renewal Fund in accordance with the Government Order on guidelines
for the State Renewal Fund; (e) implement the Voluntary Retirement Scheme in a manner satisfactory to the Bank; (f) take all the
necessary actions to ensure that employees receiving benefits under the Voluntary Retirement Scheme or the Industrial Dispute
Act are not reemployed in public undertakings or in civil service of AP; (g) implement the social safety-net program in
accordance with arrangements and procedures satisfactory to the Bank; (i) implement the technical assistance program to
strengthen institutional capacity in AP in accordance with arrangements and procedures satisfactory to the Bank; and (j)
formulate the Second Phase of the Restructuring Program by September 30, 2000.
Implementation Arrangements. GOAP shall: (a) prepare and submit to the Bank no later than June 30, 2000 a fiscal reform
program, satisfactory to the Bank, setting out, inter alia, detailed additional actions and measures to be implemented by GOAP to
achieve the objectives of the Program; (b) by December 31 of each year beginning with 1998, review with the Bank the progress
made in implementation of the fiscal program and the project as a whole against the agreed benchmarks; and (c) by September
30, 2000, undertake a Mid-Term Review with the Bank.
H. Readiness for Implementation
[ x] The engineering design documents for the first year's activities are complete and ready for the start of project
implementation.
[ x] The procurement documents for the first year's activities are complete and ready for the start of project implementation.
[ x] The Project Implementation Plan has been appraised and found to be realistic and of satisfactory quality.
