22...   The impact of irrigation investments on production would occur gradually, allowing for a probable lag in
farmer response. Total incremental production has been estimated on the basis of crop budgets per ha by region and
by irrigation scheme. Incremental production has been calculated over current levels. If the current poor
management continued over the long term, it is in fact more than likely that agricultural production would follow a
declining trend. Thus the current analysis is based on very conservative estimates of agricultural impact.
Methodology and Main Assumnptions
23.    Impact. The economic impact of the irrigation component as a whole was quantified, covering the total
project area of some 3.0 million ha, including 2.3 million ha in the Coastal Region, 0.2 million ha in the
Rayalaseema Region and 0.5 rnillion ha in the Telangana Region. Representative models of farms in the major,
medium and minor irrigation schemes for each of the three regions were developed, as the basis for estimating
economic impact at the scheme and at the aggregate levels. The combined economic impact the first three irrigation
sub-components (Scheme Performance Improvement, Institutional Support and Irrigated Agricultural Intensification



Annex 4
Page 6 of 8
Program) was also estimated on five representative schemes in different regions of the State (the Nagarjunasagar
major scheme in the Coastal Region-428,434 ha-; the Coastal Region medium scheme-6,000 ha-; the Telangana
medium scheme-4,100 ha-; the Coastal Region minor scheme-100 ha-; and the Telangana minor scheme-l 0 ha-).
The Coastal amd Telangana medium and minor schemes are regionally representative models of schemes designed
to illustrate examples of farm areas with the highest and lowest productive potential in the State. In addition, the
vast majority (87 percent) of the medium and minor irrigation schemes are located in the Coastal and Telangana
Regions. The value of incremental production for these schemes was estimated using crop budgets per ha expressed
in economic terms.
24.    The economic analysis of the SIFT component was performed separately to examine the impact of the pilot
operation. The analysis was based on the assumptions that: (a) the total irrigated area at project completion would
increase by about 20 percent in the SIFT over the without project situation; and (b) the net value of production per
ha would be 10 percent higher than those used for the project as a whole. These assumptions were made because of
the effort that would be undertaken under the SIFT component to introduce new technologies (e.g. canal
automation) as a pilot operation in selected major and medium schemes covering a total of about 46,756 ha. SIFT
investment costs (excluding agricultural intensification and institutional support) per ha were estimated at about
Rs.24,200 per ha, well above the corresponding figure (i.e., rehabilitation) of about Rs. 1,860 per ha for the project
excluding SIFT. The value of the incremental agricultural production for the SIFT was estimated using crop budgets
per ha expressed in economic terms.
25.    Costs The cost of on-going civil works, technical assistance for the preparation of the PIP, the training
modules required for initiating the MIS, and the purchase of computers and office equipment required for project
launching were based on the actual tender costs. The costs of new civil works for both the rehabilitation and
recurrent maintenance works were based on best estimates and detailed engineering work conducted by ESCI
consultants. The cost of the minimum rehabilitation works was converted to economic costs by using a specific
conversion factor of 0.86, while the recurrent maintenance works, which require a mixture of equipment, material,
fuel, skilled and unskilled labor, were converted to economic costs by using a specific conversion factor of 0.88. A
Standard Conversion Factor (SCF) of 0.90 was applied to the remaining cost items. Incremental O&M in economic
terms were derived from the project cost tables. These were separately listed in the ERR analysis along with the
cost of the rehabilitation works.
22.    ReplLacement costs, estimated at Rs.450/ha, Rs.315/ha and Rs. 180/ha for the major, medium and minor
schemes respectively, were included in the analysis in line with the following schedule: 20 percent in year 11, 30
percent in year 12, and 50 percent in year 13. To approximate the required investments after year 20 to sustain the
life of the irrigation system further, an average replacement cost per year was included in year 20.
23.    The cost streams were based on the economic costs as calculated in the project cost tables (See Annex 12
of the Project Implementation Plans for details) and exclude taxes and price contingencies. In estimating the ERRs
for the specific irrigation schemes as indicated above, the costs of the agricultural intensification and the
institutional support components of the project were allocated proportionally according to the area of each sub-
project on the basis of the average cost per ha in economic terms as derived from the project cost tables.
24.    Yield Assumptions. Increases in yield derive from the improved reliability and timing of water delivery
and improved delivery of agricultural extension services. The yield of paddy, the most extensively cultivated crop
in the project area (over 90 percent of the total cultivated area), was conservatively assumed to increase by about 3
percent. The yields of other crops were projected to increase on average by about 5 percent to 10 percent. Crop
yields vary by crop and across regions to reflect differing productive potential in different locations. Yield
assumptions for the without project situation were based on data collected from the farmers by the Directorate of
Economics and Statistics (GOAP). Average yields for the last five years were used. For the with project situation,
the yields are best estimates of crop scientists based on experience in the State. Overall, the increased output
generated by the project largely derives from increased irrigated area with a smaller contribution of increasing
yields.
25.    Prics-traded ouut. The economic prices of paddy, sorghum, sunflower, groundnuts, cotton and
sugarcane were based on projected 2005 import/export parity prices (excluding taxes and duties) derived from the
November 1997 World Bank Commodity Price Projection and expressed in 1997 constant prices. The economic
price of paddy, following recent analysis carried out for India, was considered to be somewhere between export and



Annex 4
Page 7 of 8
import parity levels. Cotto:n was treated as exportable as recent data indicate that India is a net exporter of both
cotton lint and cotton seed meal. An import parity price was taken for sugarcane. The economic price of groundnuts
was estimated taking into consideration that India exports groundnut meal but imports oil.
26.    Prices-non-traded output. Other crops produced in the project area, for which no specific world market
reference price is obtainable (i.e. vegetables, pulses) and/or because of their limited representation in the project
area cropping pattern (i.e. sunflower), were considered as non-tradable. To derive their economic prices, their
financial prices were multiplied by the SCF of 0.9.
27.    Prices- inputs. Urea, DAP and MOP, the main fertilizers used in the project area were priced at import
parity taking into account the November 1997 World Bank Commodity Price Projections. A SCF of 0.9 was
applied to pesticides, other chemicals, organic manure and draft animals. The financial prices of seeds, which are
not subsidized and do not differ significantly from their respective economic values, were used in the economic
analysis.
28.    Labor. Average wages, by season for the Coastal, Rayalaseema and Telangana Regions were set at
Rs.32/day, Rs.27/day and Rs.30/day respectively. An overall average of Rs.30/day was used in the financial
analysis. There has been no detailed study of the shadow price of rural labor in AP. For the economic analysis
unskilled labor wages were converted to their economic prices by using a SCF of 0.90.
Results
29.    The ERR for the whole irrigation component is 38 percent, generating net benefits of Rs.9 billion. The
ERR for the different schemes range from 34 percent to 99 percent. As expected the ERR for a major scheme
Nagarjunasagar (98 percent) is significantly higher than for the medium schemes. The ERRs for the medium and
minor schemes are all above the opportunity cost of capital (assumed to be 12 percent) and exhibit regional
differences--reflecting regional variation in production potential. It should be noted that the ERRs for the medium
and minor schemes in the Telangana region provide an indication of returns in areas with lower production potential
in the State. A representative medium scheme in the Coastal Region would generate net benefits of about Rs.20
million and those of the Telangana Region about Rs.8 million over the 20 year project period. The economic rate of
return for a representative minor scheme in the Coastal Region is 99 percent generating net benefits of about
Rs.335,000, while those in the Telangana are 44 percent and about Rs.129,000 respectively.
30.    The ERR for the SIFT is 25 percent, but this derives from the higher level of investments involved in this
pilot operation. The present value of net benefits, computed at the discount rate of 12 percent, is positive and
indicates that the project as a whole as well as the individual schemes would be justified. As this would be a pilot
operation, opportunities for further increasing benefits and reducing costs would be explored during the process of
implementation. One potential source for reducing costs could derive from the demonstration effect of the pilot.
Sensitivity Analysis and Project Risks
31.    Sensitivity analysis indicates that the economic rates of return of the irrigation component and for the sub-
projects are robust to wide-ranging deviations from the base case assumptions. The switching value analysis for the
irrigation component as a whole shows that it would take a 55 percent decrease in benefits or a 146 percent increase
in investment and replacement costs to lower the economic rate of return to 12 percent. Similarly, for the SIFT
component, a 66 percent decrease in benefits or a 36 percent increase in costs would lower the economic rate of
return to 12 percent.
32.    The major risks pertain to: (a) less than 100 percent operational success of the WUAs, a key building block
of GOAP's irrigation strategy; (b) delays in implementation; (c) slower than projected farmer response to the
investment program resulting in delayed accrual of benefits and (d) implementation inefficiencies leading to higher
investment costs.
33.    The success of GOAP's irrigation strategy rests on the successful performance of the WUAs, particularly
on O&M. The impact on ERRs if only 80 percent, 70 percent and 60 percent of the WUAs become operational was
therefore investigated. The results indicate that at 70 percent and 80 percent WUA success rate, the project and its
sub-components would retain ERRs greater than the cost of capital. However, at 60 percent WUA success rate, the



Annex 4
Page 8 of 8
ERR of the SIFT would decline to 12 percent. Of the various sub-components, the SIFT pilot operation exhibits the
greatest sensitivity to WUA performance.

34.    Implementation delays could arise as a result of weak implementation, difficulties in the transition into
joint I&CADD and WUA management, and/or inadequate or delayed provision by GOAP of the counterpart
funding. The impact of possible delays in implementation was examined by spreading the investments over seven
years instead of five years and lagging benefits I year over and above the base case. The ERR results indicate that
the whole irrigation component would decline to 28 percent. The ERR of the SIFT would decline to 15 percent,
while the ERRs of the various schemes would decline to 33 percent to 80 percent.
35.     The combined impact of a 70 percent WVUA success rate and implementation delays indicate that the
whole irrigation component and its various schemes maintain their good economic standing. The ERR for the
irrigation component as a whole would be 23 percent and the individual schemes ranging from 16 percent to 51
percent. The SIFT component, however, would decline to 9 percent.
36.    The impact on ERRs of delayed farmer and farm output response to the investments as well as increasing
investment costs was investigated. The whole irrigation component, the SIFT and the various irrigation schemes
would holcl up well to delaying benefits by I or 2 years. The ERR of the whole irrigation component would decline
to 27 percent as a result of a I year delay and to 21 percent as a result of a two year delay. Despite increasing
investment costs by 25 percent and 50 percent respectively, the ERRs of the whole irrigation component as well as
the individual schemes are all above the cost of capital. It is only in the serious case when benefits are lagged 2
years and investment costs are increased by 25 percent that the SIFT component ERR (11 percent) would fall
slightly below the cost of capital. The whole irrigation component would still have an ERR of 18 percent.
37.     Overall, the project is robust to expected risks, as indicated by the results of the various scenarios examined
above. The base case ERR is itself based on modest increases in irrigated area and yield impacts. Recognizing that
a state-wide program such as this would entail varying performance by WUAs and irrigation schemes, average
estimated benefits have already accounted for expected mixed performance. The state-wide estimates are
significantly lower than results obtained under pilot programs. A 30 percent shortfall in WUA performance
combined with implementation delay, that would provide a reasonable assessment of downside risks, still results in
an overall ERR of 23 percent.

Procurement and Disbursement Arrangements
PROCUREMENT
1.     Procurement methods: (Table A)
1.1.    Civil Works ($415.3m)
1.1.1   Civil works include: (i) minimum rehabilitation works to be carried out in 10 major and 63 medium
irrigation schemes including some building repairs; (ii) recurrent maintenance works to be carried out in 12,294
minor schemes in addition tD the major and medium irrigation schemes as above; (iii) system improvement works to
be carried out under the SIFT component in parts of three major schemes and in three medium schemes; (iv) repairs
and refurbishing of some INCAD buildings located in the project areas; (v) rehabilitation and upgrading of existing
rural roads and construction of bridges over major water courses; (vi) periodic maintenance works; (vii) pilot
demonstration contracts for area routine maintenance; (viii) maintenance of rural roads; (ix) construction of village
anganwadi centers each center costing around $4,000; (x) construction of 445 new buildings for PHC; (xi)
renovation of 573 PHCs ancl 48 upgraded PHCs; and (xii) construction of new primary schools (3,969) and
providing additional (3,289) class rooms. Civil works would be procured through National Competitive Bidding
(NCB) or Force Account procedures as per the bank Procurement Guidelines, January 1995, revised January and
August 1996 and September 1997.
1.1.2   NCB ($ 232.2m): The following works would follow the NCB procedure -- (i) 100% of the minimum
rehabilitation of irrigation works to be implemented at the scheme level; (ii) about 50% of the minimum
rehabilitation of irrigation works; (iii) about 90% of the SIFT works carried out in parts of three major schemes
covering 38,479 ha and three medium schemes covering 8277 ha; (iv) about 50% of recurrent maintenance works at
the scheme and distributory levels; (v) works for rehabilitation and upgrading of existing rural roads and
construction of bridges; (vi) periodic maintenance works for state roads; (vii) works for maintenance of rural roads;
(viii) renovation and/or construction of 500 upgraded PHCs; and (ix) renovation and/or construction of 450 PHCs.
The irrigation works would be grouped in workable packages for bidding not exceeding $5.0m. The road works
and minor bridge works would be grouped into packages of the size $0.6 to 1 .Om. These works would be
implemented in three phases. Periodic maintenance works of state roads would be grouped in packages of $0.5 to
$2.5m. Works for maintenance of rural roads would consist of routine maintenance contracts of small value each
less than $0.1 million and covering about 100 to 200 kms. roads and periodic maintenance contracts of size $0.1 to
0.5m. The works for construction of new PHCs and renovation of PHCs and upgraded PHCs would be suitably
packaged. Foreign firms would not find the above contracts attractive in view of the very nature of the works and
their large geographical dist-ibution. Nonetheless, bids from foreign contractors would not be precluded. Strict
post qualification criteria would be incorporated in the bidding document to ensure proper selection of contractors.
1.1.3   Force Account : ($160.1m): Civil works contracts of value less than $30,000 up to an aggregate of
$160.lm would be executed following Force Account procedures. These include the following:
(a)    Irrigation Component : Civil works to be implemented by the Water User Associations (WUAs), as well as
Scheme and Distributory committees duly constituted under the Farmers Management of Irrigation Schemes Act,
1997 would follow the Force Account procedure. These would include about (i) about 50% of the minimum
rehabilitation works; (ii) 10% of the SIFT works carried out in parts of three major schemes covering 38,479 ha and
three medium schemes covering 8,277 ha; and (iii) about 20% of the recurrent maintenance works to be
implemented by the I&CAD Department and Farmers Organizations without the assistance of the project. As
Farmers Organizations would be required to contribute 15% of the cost of civil works and 100% of O&M works,
these works would normally be implemented by these Organizations with their own labor force or through local
masons, or any other method on the basis of implementation plans approved by the I&CAD Department. In special
circumstances, such as a special request by the WUAs or when WUAs are unable to implement the approved
implementation plans, the I&CAD Department may carry out these works on behalf of the WUAs according to the
Force Account procedures-



Annex 6
Page 2 of 16
(b)    Qther Components: The following works would also be executed following other non NCB procedures:
(i) Pilot demonstration contracts for area routine maintenance with an aggregate value of $3.3m; (ii) Construction of
village Anganwadi centers, each center costing about $4,000; (iii) Some renovation contracts for the upgraded
PHCs and PHCs; and (iv) Construction of primary schools.
(c)    Other non-NCB procedures would include contracts awarded: (i) on the basis of comparison of price
quotations obtained from at least three qualified contractors eligible under the Guidelines or registered NGOs,
WUAs (or other beneficiary organizations), School Construction Committee/Village Education Committee/Parent
Teacher Association/Block Resource Committee/Mothers' Committee; or (ii) on the basis of unit/piece rate systems
to qualified contractors or registered NGOs, WUAs (or other beneficiary organizations), School Construction
Committee/Village Education Committee/Parent Teacher Association/Block Resource Committee/Mothers'
Committee; or (iii) direct contracting to NGOs or WUAs (or other beneficiary organizations), School Construction
Committee/Village Education Committee/Parent Teacher Association/Block Resource Committee/Mothers'
Committee; or (iv) by Force Account as a last resort in a manner satisfactory to the Association in accordance with
Paragraph 3.8 of the Guidelines.
1.1.2   Bidding Documents: Standard Bidding Documents as finalized by the Government of India Task Force
and agreed with the Bank would be used for all NCB contracts. Contracts with the WUAs and Scheme and
Distributory Committees; Contracts with the School Construction Committee/Village Education Committee/Parent
Teacher Association/Block Resource Committee/Mothers' Committee; and contracts with registered contractors by
collecting quotations would be concluded based on the formats developed by NDO Procurement Unit and cleared
with the Bank.
1.2.    Goods and Equipment: ($59.4m)
1.2.1   The project supports the procurement of following component under the Irrigation Component: (i)
communication and computing equipment for the MIS; (ii) canal automation equipment for the SIFT component;
(iii) weather stations, water measuring devices, as well as topographical instruments for monitoring and evaluation,
training equipment and material for HRD unit, and some office equipment and furniture for the PMU, HRD unit,
Irrigated Agriculture Coordination Unit, and the WALAMATARI training and research Institute; and (iv) vehicle
refurbishing.
In the case of the other components, the project supports the procurement of furniture, equipment (office and other),
vehicles, imedicines, MIS/IEC materials, books and library requirements, educational and teaching materials
(including contingency funds at MRC/school) and other supplies.
1.2.2  International Competitive Bidding (ICB): ($8.4 m). Contracts for the purchase of Goods and Equipment
valued at $200,000 or more would be procured through International Competitive Bidding (ICB) procedures in
accordance with Bank Procurement Guidelines, January 1995, revised January and August 1996, and September
1997. Communications and computer hardware ($2.4m), and canal automation equipment ($6.0), would be
procured through ICB.
1.2.3  Nqational Competitive Bidding (NCB): ($24.OmL. Contracts for the purchase of Goods and Equipment,
medicines, educational and teaching materials, MIS/IEC materials and other supplies valued less than $200,000 per
contract vvould be procured under contracts awarded on the basis of NCB procedures in accordance with the
provisions of Paragraphs 3.3 and 3.4 of the Guidelines.
1.2.4 Nqational Shopping: (S23.0m). Office equipment, furniture, small equipment, medicines, books and library,
educational and teaching materials, MIS/IEC materials, other supplies estimated to cost less than the equivalent of
$30,000 per contract up to an aggregate not to exceed $23.0 M may be procured under contracts awarded on the
basis of Shopping procedures in accordance with the provisions of Paragraphs 3.5 and 3.6 of the Guidelines.
1.2.5   Yehicles and Vehicle Refurbishing: ($1.0 m). Vehicles estimated to cost not more than $100,000 per
contract and $500,000 in the aggregate may be procured under contracts awarded on the basis of national shopping
procedures in accordance with paragraphs 3.5 and 3.6 of the Guidelines. Vehicle refurbishing estimated to cost not

1.2.6   Books. CD ROMs. Periodicals etc: ($3.0m). Books, CD ROMs, software, some educational and teaching
materials, furniture, software, audio and video cassettes and proprietary equipment including spares for already
available equipment and proprietary medicines, MIS/lEC materials, extension and publicity materials aggregating to
$3.Om may be procured on dlirect contracting procedures in accordance with Paragraph 3.7 of the Guidelines.
1.2.7   Bidding Documents: Standard Bidding Documents as finalized by the Government of India Task Force and
agreed with the Bank would be used for all ICB, and NCB contracts. For Shopping contracts the format as
developed by NDO Procurernent Unit would be used.
1.3    TechnicalAssistance, Studies and Training: ($94.5m)
1.3.1   Technical Assistance and Consultancy Services would be contracted following procedures in accordance
with Bank Guidelines for Sel[ection and Employment of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers, January 1997,
revised September 1997. The Standard Request for Proposals and Conditions of Contract would be used for all
contracts. The list of major consultancies and the proposed method of selection of these consultancies is as per
Table C.
1.3.2   Training (Agriculture Department): Training of Agriculture Department (AD) staff, trainers and WUA
motivators would be conducted at AD training institutes, District Training Centers and selected places by the AD.
In case of training by Agriculture Universities, the method of procurement shall be sole source contracting on basis
of training plan and cost estimates satisfactory to the Bank. Domestic study tours would be arranged by the
concerned training institutes or the AD.
1.3.3   Training (I&CAD  lepartment): Training of the I&CAD Department staff, trainers, NGOs, and WUA
functionaries and facilitators, would be conducted at WALAMTARI, District Training Units and selected places by
the I&CAD Department. Domestic study tours would be arranged by WALAMTARI or the Department.
1.3.4   Training (Primaryvi'[ealth Component): Training would be arranged for management training as well as to
enhance clinical and technical training to improve the quality of services. Local and international fellowships are
included.
1.3.5   Training services in established institutions would be procured directly subject to clearance with Bank on
course content, number and details of personnel to be deputed, and course fee. Other training services would be
procured following Quality and Cost Based Selection [QCBS] procedure.
1.4    Miscellaneous: ($260.4m)
1.4.1   Contracts for maintenance of irrigation schemes, buildings, vehicles and equipment costing less than
$30,000 would be procured either through Force Account or Direct Contracting. Materials required for 0 & M of
buildings and 'Others, individually costing the equivalent of US $10 ,000 and less per contract would be procured
following Shopping procedures. The additional expenditures incurred by AP Housing Corporation Ltd. and AP
Health and Medical Housing and Infrastructure Development Corporation Ltd. for providing technical services in
civil works' construction for ICDS and Primary Health Components would be reimbursed under Incremental
Operating Costs.
Under Public Enterprise Reform, 'Severance Cost' would be financed under SOE.

2.     Review by the Bank of Procurement Decisions: (Table B)
2.1    Procurement Planning
Prior to the issuance of any invitations to prequalify for bidding or to bid for contracts, the proposed procurement
plan for the project shall be furnished to the Bank for its review and approval, in accordance with the provisions of
paragraph 1 of Appendix I to the Guidelines. Procurement of all goods and works shall be undertaken in
accordance with procurement plan as shall have been approved by the Bank and with the provisions of said
paragraph 1.
2.2    Prior Review
2.2.1   Civil Works: All packages for civil works exceeding the equivalent of $300,000 or more would be fully
documentedl and subject to prior review by the Bank as per procedures set forth in paragraphs 2 and 3 of Appendix
I to the Bank Guidelines.
2.2.2   Goods and Equipment: All ICB packages irrespective of value would be subject to prior review by the
Bank. NCB packages of value $200,000 or more would be subject to prior review.
2.2.3   Technical Assistance. Studies and Training: The following would be subject to prior review by the Bank:
(a)    Terms of References for all consultancies;
(b)    Contracts for the employment of consulting firms estimated to cost more than $100,000;
(c)    Contracts for the employment of Individuals estimated to cost more than $50,000;
(d)    All single source selection of consulting firms/organizations;
(e)    Amendments to contracts for the employment of consulting firms raising the contract value to
$100,000 or above;
(f)    Assignments of critical nature, as reasonably determined by the Bank; and
(g)    Amendments to contracts for the employment of individual consultants raising the contract value
to $50,000 or above.
2.3    Post Review
2.3.1   Works and Goods: The contracts below the prior review threshold for Works and Goods shall be subject to
post review as per procedure set forth in paragraph 4 of Appendix I of the Bank Guidelines.
2.3.2   Te hnical Assistance, Studies and Training: Contracts for the employment of consulting firms estimated to
cost less than $100,000 and contracts for the employment of individuals estimated to cost less than $50,000 shall be
subject to post review provided that the TORs have been cleared with the Bank.
2.4    Procurement Information
Procurement Information would be collected and recorded as follows:
(a)    Prompt reporting of contract award information by Project Monitoring Unit [PMU] of
Government of AP [GOAP].
(b)    Comprehensive semi-annual reports by PMU of GOAP indicating:
(i)    revised cost estimates for individual contracts and total cost;
(ii)    revised timings of procurement actions including advertising, bidding, contract award
and completion time for individual contracts; and
(iii)   compliance with aggregate limits on the specified methods of procurement.
(c)    Completion report by the borrower within three months of the credit closing date.

3.     Disbursement
3.1    The proposed IDA Credit of SDR 179.4 million (US$ 241.9 million equivalent) would finance
expenditures incurred under Primary Education, Nutrition (ICDS), and partial expenditures (US$ 30 million
equivalent) under the Health component. The proposed IBRD Loan of US$ 301.3 million would finance
expenditures incurred under Irrigation, Roads, Public Sector Reform components and partial expenditures under the
Health component. The Credit/Loan would be disbursed in accordance with Table D of this annex. Disbursements
are projected over a period of 5 1/2 years from July 1998 for expenditures incurred/to be incurred over a period of
5 years. Project completion is expected by 30-September-2003 and Credit/Loan closing by 3 1-March-2004. All
requests for disbursements would be prepared by the Finance and Accounts professional in each implementing
department with a copy to PMU for monitoring and consolidation. Disbursements for civil works contracts
exceeding US$ 300,000 or rnore would be fully documented and subject to prior review by the Bank.
Disbursements for goods and equipment contracts exceeding US$ 200,000 and all ICB contracts irrespective of the
value of the contract would be fully documented. For consultants, this limit would be US$ 100,000 for firms and
US$ 50,000 for individuals. For all other expenditures, Credit/Loan funds would be disbursed against Statement of
Expenditures (SOEs) in an agreed format and in accordance with the Bank's normal procedure for SOEs. SOEs
would be certified by the implementing departments as representing eligible project expenditures. Supporting
documents for SOEs would be available for post-review by supervision missions and auditors, and retained by the
implementing departments for one year after the receipt of audit report by the Bank for the fiscal year in which the
last withdrawal from the Credit/Loan was made.
Retroactive Financing and Special Account
3.2    To assist with project preparation and to expedite project start-up, IDA/Bank would finance project costs
up to a limit of US$ 50 million, provided IDA/Bank procurement procedures were followed and the expenditures



Annex 6
Page 14 of 16
were incurred after l-August-1997. The activities to be financed on a retroactive basis would cover all the
components. However, in the following components it would include: (a) in irrigation component expenditures
incurred on project activities including studies and scheme recurrent costs; (b) in roads component on activities for
initiating emergency and periodic maintenance, and consulting services; (c) in primary health component
expenditures incurred on preparation activities including facilities and equipment surveys, preparation of
procurement documents and model plans, studies, information technology equipment, vehicles and related
operational expenditures. In order to facilitate timely payments of project expenditures, a Special Account
amounting to US$ 13 million for IDA financed components and US$ 17 millions for IBRD financed components
would be established in the Reserve Bank of India. The Special Account would be replenished on the basis of
Withdrawal Applications for eligible expenditures approved by the Bank. The proceeds out of the Credit/Loan
would be passed on to GOAP by GOI under its standard policy on release of external assistance funds to the states.

Annex 12

ANNEX 12
THE FISCAL FRAMEWORK
1.     Since the mid-1980s AP has been under substantial fiscal stress created by (a) declining own revenue of the
State as a share of GSDP, and (b) sharply rising public expenditures on subsidies, salaries, and poorly targeted
welfare programs, which have crowded out productive expenditure on infrastructure, social sectors, and non-wage
O&M. The situation were aggravated in 1994-94 and 1994-95 by introduction of total prohibition of sale of
alcoholic drinks and increased subsidization of rice distribution under the 2-rupee rice scheme. These two schemes,
which have imposed a burden of about 3 percent of GSDP on the State's budget, led to an unprecedented fiscal
crisis in 1994-95 and 1995-96. The spending plans of the Government were forced to fall in line with the resource
constraint. The burden of adjustment fell on productive expenditure while subsidies and salaries were largely
protected.
2.     The present Goveniment which took office in December 1995, has taken several politically difficult fiscal
and sector measures to reverse these trends including reduction in rice subsidy and staff in the State Government,
partial relaxation of prohibition on the sale of alcoholic beverages, introduction of a public enterprise reform which
has so far achieved privatization of two public enterprises and closure of four unviable companies, threefold
increase in irrigation charges, and enactment of a Power Sector Reform Bill which would trigger a series of
fundamental reforms in the sector. These measures constitute a sharp break from the earlier populist policies and a
significant step towards redefining the role of government in AP - reducing it where it can be efficiently replaced
by the private sector, and increasing it in the provision of essential public services such as primary education,
primary health, and road maintenance.
3.     The fiscal restructuring program of the Government for the next five years, supported by APERP, is a
continuation of the measures already taken in a broader fiscal framework. This framework is summarized after a
brief description of the main fiscal developments since the early 1990s. More detailed description and analysis of
the fiscal developments in a longer perspective can be found in the following documents on the project file: (a)
White Papers and the Fiscal Policy Statement of GOAP; (b) Bank's report "AP: Agenda for Economic Reforms"
(Report No: 15901 -IN, January 16, 1997); and (c) Aide memoire of the appraisal mission (April 6, 1998).
A BRIEF' REVIEW OF THE RECENT FISCAL DEVELOPMENTS
4.     From the point of vitew of fiscal stance, the 1990s can be divided into two periods. The first period (from
1990-91 to 1994-95) is characterized by serious fiscal deterioration. The second period (from 1995-96 to present)
shows signs of fiscal recovery. In the first period, most fiscal indicators deteriorated (Table 1); the fiscal deficit
increased from 3.3 percent of GSDP in 1990-91 to 3.8 percent in 1994-95, the revenue deficit and the primary
deficit rose from 0.5 percent and 1.1 percent of GSDP to 1.2 percent and 1.8 percent, respectively, in the same
period, and composition of expenditure deteriorated (The share interest payments, the salary bill, and subsidies
increased at the expense of productive expenditure on O&M, capital expenditure, and education and health). The
deterioration can be ascribed to the structural weaknesses in state finances (inelastic revenue system, rapid increase
in employment in the State Government, proliferation of subsidy programs, etc.) and, particularly, introduction of
total prohibition of sale of alcoholic drinks and increased subsidization of rice distribution by the previous
Government.
5.     The fiscal situation has improved markedly in the second period. The fiscal deficit has been brought down
from 3.8 percent of GSDP in 1994-95 to an estimated 3.0 percent in 1997-98. The revenue deficit and the primary
deficit have also been reduced from 1.2 percent and 1.8 percent of GSDP to 0.3 percent and 0.7 percent,
respectively, in the same period. Successful revenue mobilization has been a key factor in bringing about this
consistent improvement in fiscal situation. Both the State's own revenue and transfers from the Central
Government have increased in this period; 0.6 percent of GSDP in the case of own revenue and 0.4 percent in
transfers. The resulting increase in total revenue of I percent of GSDP enabled the Government to achieve a 0.8
point reduction in fiscal deficit while increasing total expenditure by 0.2 points.
6.     The own revenue increase of 0.6 percent of GSDP was a result of a 0.8 point increase in tax revenue and a
0.2 point fall in non-tax revenue. The revenue efforts such as introduction of a turnover tax and an increase in the



Annex 12
Page 2 of 9
motor vehicle entry tax are responsible for the own tax increase'. Decline in non-tax revenue is explained by the
fact that charges for many public services are on ad valorem basis, which are not adjusted frequently to keep pace
with nominal GSDP.

7.      Several steps have been taken in the second period to restructure the composition of expenditure. The rice
subsidy has been reduced by 41 percent from Rs. 1125 crores in 1995-96 to Rs.660 crores in 1997-98 (or, from 1.6
percent of GSDP to 0.7 percent). This was achieved by a large increase in the sale price and some reduction in the
entitlement per family in July 1996. Strong measures have also been introduced since 95, including freeze on
hiring, to contain sharp increase in employment in the State Government. It is estimated that these measures have
reduced employment by about 3 percent in the past three years and changed its composition in favor of social
sectors. As a result, the salary bill was reduced from 6.2 percent of GSDP in 1994-95 to 5.3 percent in 1997-98.
8.      A worrying development is that the budgetary savings from the reduction in rice subsidy and employment
have been absorbed by other unproductive expenditure. In particular, interest payments have increased from 2.0
percent of (iSDP in 1994-95 to 2.4 percent in 1997-98, reflecting higher real interest rates following partial
liberalization of the lending rates and a fall in inflation in recent years2. Pension payments have also increased from
1 A sharp fall in tax revenue in 1995-96 and an equally sharp recovery in 1997-98 is explained by introduction of
prohibition on the sale of alcoholic beverages and partial reversal of this policy in 1997-98.
2 The average real interest rate on government debt was estimated at 0.5 percent in the period from 1990-91 to
1993-94 and 3.4 percent since then. The average nominal rate in year t was estimated by dividing total interest
payments in year t+l by the outstanding debt in year t. All India CPI was used to calculate the real rates.



Annex 12
Page 3 of 9
1.2 percent of GSDP in 1994-95 to 1.4 percent in 1997-98 partly offsetting the savings from employment reduction.
In addition, net loans and advances to APSEB have increased from 0.7 percent of GSDP in 1994-95 to 1.3 percent
in 1997-98. These loans are, in effect, indirect subsidies because they are used to finance the operating losses of
APSEB and, in later years, converted into grant or equity. An increase in these loans crowds out public investment
in infrastructure, because they are part of the capital expenditure of the budget.
9.     These developments indicate that containing the power sector subsidies and interest payments is critical in
achieving a notable change in expenditure composition, and that state finances cannot be made sustainable unless
the power sector is reformed and the fiscal deficit is further reduced to a sustainable level. The Government reform
program for the next 5-6 years -- described in the Letter of Development Policy (Annex 13) -- aims to address
particularly these two issues, while broadening and deepening the fiscal and sector reforms already initiated. The
proposed APERP and the power sector operation, which are being processed in parallel, are the main instruments
with which the Bank supports the Government to achieve these objectives.
10.    Fiscal Outturn in 1997-98. Fiscal improvements continued in 1997-98. The fiscal outturn was markedly
better than expected. With a revenue increase of 1.1 percent of GSDP lead by excise and sales taxes as a result the
relaxation of prohibition, and an expenditure increase of 0.7 percent of GSDP, the fiscal deficit declined by 0.4
percentage points to 3.0 percent against the budgeted 3.5 percent. Both the revenue deficit and primary deficit also
declined markedly.
11.    The 1998-99 Budget. Against this background, the 1998-99 budget aims to consolidate the earlier gains in
fiscal adjustment while maintaining the reform momentum. The fiscal deficit is budgeted to stay at the present level
of 3.0 percent of GSDP1. The expected revenue increase of 0.7 percent of GSDP (0.4 percent in own tax revenue
and 0.3 percent in transfers fromn the center which partly reflects the inflow of APERP funds) would finance partly
capital outlays and partly interest payments. The salary bill is projected to remain at 5.3 percent of GSDP despite
the planned reduction of employment in the State Government (see below) because the Government intends to fill
27,000 vacancies in the primary education sector in 1998-99. Expenditure on O&M is budgeted to increase from
1.2 percent of GSDP to 1.3 percent supported by APERP. Net loans to APSEB in 1998-99 is budgeted at 0.9
percent of GSDP, down from 1.3 percent in 1997-98, reflecting expected improvement in APSEB's financial
performance under the ongoing reform program. Capital outlays is budgeted to increase from 1.1 percent of GSDP
to 1.3 percent. The revenue deficit is estimated to remain very low at 0.4 percent of GSDP in 1998-99 (slightly up
from 0.3 percent of GSDP), and the primary deficit would fall further from 0.7 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 0.4
percent in 1998-99.
THE FISCAL PROGRAM
Fiscal Objectives
12.    The fiscal restructuring program of the Government for the period 1998-99 to 2002-03 builds on the
progress already made in the past three years. It has three main objectives:
* Changing the composition of public expenditure, which would involve a fall in the share of untargeted
subsidies, the salary bill, and interest payments in total expenditure and an increase in the share of expenditure
on operation and maintenance, social sectors, and infrastructure. This would significantly improve the
development impact of public expenditure and ensure that adequate budgetary funds are available to implement
the investments APERP supports. Quantitative targets for expenditure composition have been worked out and
agreed upon with the state aiuthorities (see below). Restructuring of public expenditure also signify a new
antipoverty strategy based on acceleration of growth and employment creation, with smaller but better targeted
subsidy programs for the very poor.
* Containing the growth of GOAP' s debt and guarantees. This would require stabilizing the debt/GSDP and the
guarantees/GSDP ratios at about the 24 percent and 9 percent, respectively. This would, in turn, help stabilize
the share of interest payments in revenue at about 18 percent (These three ratios are below the average of all
The impact of APERL is estimated and incorporated in the 1998-99 Budget presented in Table 1.



Annex 12
Page 4 of 9
states). The main fiscal implication of this objective is a further reduction in fiscal deficit from the present 3.0
of GSDE' to 2.5 percent by the end of the program. Annual deficit targets have been projected for the program
period atid agreed upon with the state authorities. Reduction in fiscal deficit would not be smooth and
continuous. In fact, the deficit is projected to deteriorate in 1999-00 when pay increase is expected to be
awarded (see below). Projections show that fiscal deficit would gradually fall to 2.5 percent of GSDP in 2002-
03 after going up to 3.4 percent in 1999-00.
*  Enhancing the revenue efforts, including an improvement of the tax system that broadens the revenue base and
increases buoyancy and efficiency through simplification and other administrative measures, and stronger cost
recovery. The specific measures include: preparation for a VAT to replace the present sales tax under technical
assistance funded by DFID of the UK Government, and enhanced cost recovery in canal irrigation.
Policy Measures
13.    The Government intends to take the following measures during the program period to achieve these
objectives.
* Staffreduction: The Government would continue reducing employment in the State Government and changing
its composition. To be able to accommodate the expected increase of about 52,000 new teachers in primary
education under DPEP and others which may be created by the Central Government under the centrally
sponsored schemes, the primary education sector would not be included in the staff reduction program. For the
rest of the sectors, an annual staff reduction target of 1.9 percent has been agreed. This would enable the
Govermnent to reduce employment in these sectors by 76,000 in five years (from 834,000 in 1997-98 to
758,000 in 2002-03), with natural attrition (Table 2). The rate of natural attrition is estimated at 2.1 percent.
Under this plan, total employment in the State Government is estimated to fall by 2.5 percent over the next five
years (about 0.5 percent a year). In addition, the composition of employment would change significantly. For
example, the share of primary education teachers in total employment would increase from 15 percent in 1997-
98 to 24 percent in 2002-03. In view of the rapid increase in pension payments, a paper would be prepared to
explore the options for a pension refonn in AP.

*Rice subsidy: The Government has already reduced rice subsidy by 41 percent from Rs.1,125 crores in 1995-
96 to Rs.660 crores in 1997-98 (or, from 1.6 percent of GSDP to 0.7 percent) by raising the sale price and
reducing the entitlement per family. The program now covers about 70 percent of the population. Further
rationalization of the scheme and better targeting are planned for. In particular, (a) to improve targeting, the
bogus (illegal) ration cards would be eliminated; (b) the design and implementation of the scheme would be



Annex 12
Page 5 of 9
improved to better target the subsidy to the poor; and (c) the existing food committees at all levels would be
strengthened to increase representation of the women's groups in order to better monitor the program from the
perspective of beneficiaries. In addition, a study would be undertaken to assess the impact of controls on rice
sales on the AP economy in general and on farmers in particular.
*   Irrigation: Irrigation charges were increased threefold in January 1997. They cover about 70 percent of the
required O&M costs. However, the collection rate is 60 percent. The Government intends to strengthen
revenue collection. The reform agenda includes: (a) the collection rate would be targeted at achieving at least
90 percent by March 31, 2001; (b) the Water Charges Review Committee (WCRC) would be fully
operationalized to make annual recommendations regarding the water rates to be levied and expenditure
requirements for O&M, (c) cost sharing of 15 percent for investment costs in minimum rehabilitation and SIFT
works in the area of operation of WUAs in cash and or kind, would be introduced; and (d) progress would be
made, on a pilot basis, towards introduction of volumetric charges for water and collection of water charges by
WUAs.
*   Tax measures: The Government intends to modernize the general sales tax system which accounts for about
two-thirds of the total tax revenue, by introducing a VAT after adequate preparation of about 2 years.
Preparation would be supported under a technical assistance program to be funded by DFID. A 5-day
workshop on VAT would be held in Hyderabad in late June 1998.
Replacement of the sales tax with a VAT would significantly improve the efficiency in resource allocation in
the State. By broadening the tax base, it would also have a positive impact on revenue. However, in conducting
fiscal projections for the program period (see below), it was assumed that introduction of VAT would be
revenue neutral. That is, the fiscal benefits of the improved tax system would be passed on to the tax payers in
terms of lower rates.
*   Expenditure management: The Government intends to improve the expenditure management. The following
would be the main elements of the program: (a) emphasis on better maintenance of the existing assets in
preference to starting new projects; (b) completion of the ongoing projects through critical balancing
investment before making fresh commitments; (c) selection of new projects and schemes on strict cost-benefit
criteria while taking into account both positive and negative externalities; (d) zero-based budgeting to evaluate
every scheme and phasing out ineffective ones; and (e) budgeting based on a realistic estimate of resources so
as not to overstretch comnmitments. Technical assistance would be identified to support the Government efforts.
Fiscal Impact of the Expected Pay Increase
14.    Pay policy in AP Government is not formally linked to the Central Government policies. Therefore, pay
adjustment in AP normally follows a different timetable and rate increase. Past practice indicate that roughly in
every five years, based partly on the recommendations of a state-level Pay Commission, a major increase has been
given. In between, salaries are adjusted for inflation with a structural increase of 1-2 percent in real terms. The last
pay increase in AP was about 115 percent in 1994. Following the recent large salary increase at the Center, the State
Government set up a Pay Commission to determine what additional increase would be needed. The Pay
Commission is expected to submit its recommendations by the end of 1998. Based on these recommendations and
negotiations with the staff, it is expected that the Government would announce the increase in 1999 before State
elections in November. The size of the increase and the way it is financed would have important implications for
the fiscal program.
15.    At this time, it is not possible to determine what the size of the increase would be. In fiscal projections,
conducted jointly with the Finance Department, it is assumed that (a) the increase would be 20 percent (to ensure
relative parity with the increase in Central Government); (b) it would be made effective from January 1, 1998; and
(c) all payments including arrears would be paid in 1999-00. This scenario which is considered by the authorities as
the worst case scenario, is adopted to have conservative projections in the first two years of the reform program.
The projections indicate that the wage bill including pensions would increase from 6.7 percent of GSDP in 1997-98
to 8.4 percent in 1999-00. It would then gradually fall to 6.5 percent at the end of the program period (Table 2).
16.    To mitigate the impact, the Government is considering several measures including spreading the payment
over a period of time particularly after 1999-00, introducing new revenue measures or VRS in departments where



Annex 12
Page 6 of 9
excess employment is high. These decisions would have to be made at the time the pay award is negotiated with the
staff and be part of the final agreement. It was agreed however that the 1999-00 Budget should be prepared and
resources to fnance the pay increase be identified very carefully to ensure that the priority expenditure is protected
in that year. The fiscal projections show that the expected revenue increase and further savings in other expenditure
categories would not be sufficient to fully finance the assumed pay hike, and that new borrowing would be
necessary. Irhis implies that a temporary deterioration in fiscal situation in 1999-00 is unavoidable (see below).
Impact of the Fiscal Program and APERP
17.    The main measurable impact of the fiscal measures would be: (a) revenue increase from improved cost
recovery in irrigation (as noted earlier, the actual revenue increase would be marginal); (b) budgetary savings from
reduction of rice subsidy (the rice subsidy would fall from 0.7 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 0.3 percent in 2000-
01 if the coverage is reduced to 40 percent of the population); and (c) budgetary savings from staff reduction
program (the salary bill including pension payments would fall from 6.7 percent of GSDP to 6.5 percent at the end
of the program period after increasing to 8.4 percent in 1999-00. APERP itself would have three main effects.
First, APERP would increase total expenditure by the expected disbursement each year (0.34 percent of GSDP).
Second, it would increase revenue (0.2 percent of GSDP) in terms of grants from the Central Government because
some of the sector components are planned to be centrally sponsored schemes and the others passed on to the State
Government in 30:70 ratio of grant to loan. Third, it would change the composition of expenditure in a major way
through the sector projects it finances. The fiscal projections reported in the next section incorporates these effects.
Fiscal Projections
18.    Fiscal projections have been conducted in collaboration with the Finance Department to estimate the
impact of the policy measures included in the program, and determine monitorable fiscal targets for the program
period. The projections also reflect various assumptions related to inflation, GSDP growth, revenue buoyancy, etc.
(See the aid memoire of the appraisal mission for these assumptions and detailed projections). A summary of the
main results is presented in Table 1.
19.    The projections do not reflect the budgetary impact of the power sector reform program, in particular, the
financial restructuring plan for APSEB, under preparation in the context of the Bank's power sector operation.
Completion of this plan, discussion with all concerned parties, and agreement on the plan with the Bank and the
creditors is expected to take 10-12 months. The fiscal impact of this plan would be considered separately when it is
completed and agreed upon. However, to have realistic fiscal projections until the power sector reform is in place, it
is assumed in these projections that the net loans and advances to the power sector would be kept at 0.7 percent of
GSDP, down slightly from the budgeted 0.9 percent in 1998-99 in anticipation of the positive impact of the
measures being considered in the power sector.
20.    The financial restructuring plan for APSEB would estimate the funds needed for new investment by
APSEB and servicing its outstanding liabilities in a reformed power sector, and identify sources of finance. Some
of these funds need to be provided by the State Government which may be large in the initial 2-3 years of
implementation of the power sector reform program. Therefore, the fiscal deficit and some other fiscal indicators
such as the debt/GSDP ratio and the interest payments/GSDP ratio, are expected to deteriorate initially. But if the
fiscal sustainability of the power sector is attained, the positive medium-term fiscal impact of the power sector
reforms would far outweigh the initial negative impact. In fact, as noted earlier, fiscal sustainability in AP cannot be
ensured without radical power sector reforms.
21.    The pay increase is incorporated in the projections on the basis of assumptions summarized in para. 15. It
was agreed with the Finance Department that if the conditions of the actual pay settlement is fiscally more
comfortable than what is assumed (for example, the increase is lower than 20 percent, and the payment is spread
over a period of time), the fiscal projections would be adjusted accordingly during annual reviews of the program.
22.    The projections show that, with the exception of 1999-00, the overall fiscal situation would continue to
improve in the program period. In particular, after going up to 3.4 percent of GSDP in 1999-00 (reflecting the
impact of the expected pay hike), the fiscal deficit would gradually decline to 2.5 percent. If it is sustained, a 2.5
percent deficit would be one of the lowest among Indian states. The revenue balance and the primary balance would



Annex 12
Page 7 of 9
turn positive in 2000-01 and remain positive thereafter. AP would be the first state in India to achieve positive
revenue and primary balances.
23.    A key factor affecting fiscal performance is the expected revenue increase of 1.7 percent of GSDP which
would facilitate a 0.5 point reduction in fiscal deficit and a 1.2 point increase in total expenditure (mainly in the
social sectors and infrastructure). The revenue increase would come largely from the State's own taxes (one percent
of GSDP) reflecting the full impact of partial elimination of prohibition on the sale of alcoholic beverages on sales
tax and excise particularly in the first 2 years. The combined sales tax and excise revenue is projected to go up from
5.9 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 6.6 percent in 1999-00. Transfers from the Central Government are also
expected to increase by 0.6 percent of GSDP on account of the grant element in the APERP funds and the expected
implementation of the new devolution formula for the central taxes recommended by the 10th Finance Commission.
24.    The 1.2 points increase in total expenditure is projected to be divided equally between the revenue and
capital expenditure. In particular, capital outlays (capital expenditure minus net loans and advances to public
enterprises) show an expansion from 1I.1 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 2.5 percent in 2002-03, indicating
substantial increase in budgetary funds available for investment in infrastructure.
25.    The projections show that the debt/GSDP ratio and the interest payments/GSDP ratio would increase
slightly in the first four years of the program period because of higher fiscal deficit generated by the assumed large
pay increase. However, both rates would start declining in the final year. If the fiscal deficit is maintained at 2.5
percent of GSDP in later years, the debtWGSDP ratio would stabilize at 24 percent and the interest payments/GSDP
ratio at 2.9 percent, both below the average of the major states of India.
26.    The main impact of the program is on the composition of expenditvre. Allocations for non-wage O&M
would increase from 1.2 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 2.2 percent in the fourth year of the program and remain at
that level thereafter while expenditure on salaries, transfers and subsidies decrease (Table 1). Similarly, capital
outlays (investment in infrastructure) would go up from 1I.1 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 2.2 percent in 2000-01
and 2.5 percent in 2002-03. In terms of sectoral classification, the combined budgetary allocations for irrigation,
roads, primary education, and primary health would increase from 2.5 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 4.3 percent
by the end of the program period (Table 3).
The Fiscal Targets
27.    Based on the fiscal projections, agreement has been reached on the following fiscal targets for monitoring
the overall fiscal performance during the program period.
I. Reduce the fiscal deficit by 0.5 percentage point in the program period from the estimated 3.0 percent of GSDP
in 1997-98 according to the following annual targets; 3.0 percent in 1998-99, 3.4 percent in 1999-00, 2.9
percent in 2000-01, 2.7 percent in 2001-02, and 2.5 percent in 2002-03.
2. Achieve positive revenue balance of 0.4 percent of GSDP in 2000-01 and keep it at least at that level thereafter.
3. Keep the guarantees/GS]DP ratio at or below the present average 9 percent.
4. Reduce employment in the State Government (excluding primary education) by 1.9 percent a year.
5. Reduce the salary/GSDP ratio from the estimated 5.3 percent in 1997-98 to 5.0 percent in 2002-03.
6. Increase total combined expenditure in primary education and primary health from the estimated 0.9 percent of
GSDP in 1997-98 to 1.8 percent in 2002-03 (approximately 0.18 points per year).
7. Increase expenditure on non-salary O&M from the estimated 1.2 percent of GSDP in 1997-98 to 2.0 percent in
2000-01 and maintain it at least at that level thereafter.
8. Increase expenditure on capital outlays (excluding net loans and advances) from the estimated 1.1 percent of
GSDP to 2.2 percent in 2000-01 and maintain it at least at that level thereafter.



Annex 12
Page 8 of 9
9. Improve targeting by eliminating the illegally held ration cards.
10. Increase the collection rate of irrigation charges at least to 90 percent of the collectable demand and maintain it
at least at that level thereafter.

Fiscal Sustainabilitv
28.     In designing APERP, fiscal sustainability was considered at both the state and the project level. At the
state level, putting state finances on a sustainable path is a critical objective of APERP. The Government aims to
achieve this objective by (a) stabilizing the debt/GSDP ratio at about 24 percent to contain the interest burden at 18
percent of total revenue (both below the average of all Indian states). This requires a reduction of fiscal deficit from
the present 3.0 percent of GSDP to 2.5 percent by the end of the program period; (b) improving development impact
of public expenditure through changing its composition to shift resources from salaries and untargeted subsidies into
social sectors and infrastructure; and (c) simplifying the tax system and improving cost recovery to strengthen
revenue mobilization.
29.     Fiscal sustainability of the project components is equally important. A project of this size would have
significant budgetary implications both during and after the project period. For example, the primary education
component would increase the number of teachers, salaries of which would have to be paid fully from the budget
after the project is completed; food costs of the nutrition component need to be provided from the budget; and
budgetary allocations for O&M in the irrigation and road sectors which would be raised to the required norms under
the project, would need to be kept at that level in real terms in the post-project period. The question is whether
adequate budgetary funds could be made available both during and after the project period to meet the required
expenditure to sustain the investment supported by APERP without crowding out other priority expenditure.
30.     The budgetary impact of the project (i.e., local funds needed for implementation of all components) would
amount to 0.2 percent of GSDP during the project period and 0.3 percent after the project is completed. This



Annex 12
Page 9 of 9
increased budgetary burden would be manageable in the context of the fiscal reform program. It could be met
entirely from the budgetary savings from rationalization of the rice subsidy and staff in the State Government,
which would jointly constitute about 0.7 percent of GSDP at the end of the project period.
